<th class="summary" colspan="2" style="background-color:#B0C4DE;text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;font-size:110%;">Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="background-color:#DCDCDC;text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;">Part of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">Pacific War</a> of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;line-height:1.5em;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atomic_bombing_of_Japan.jpg"><img alt="Two aerial photos of atomic bomb mushroom clouds, over two Japanese cities in 1945." data-file-height="2918" data-file-width="4918" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Atomic_bombing_of_Japan.jpg/300px-Atomic_bombing_of_Japan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Atomic_bombing_of_Japan.jpg/450px-Atomic_bombing_of_Japan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Atomic_bombing_of_Japan.jpg/600px-Atomic_bombing_of_Japan.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a><br/>
Atomic bomb <a href="/wiki/Mushroom_cloud" title="Mushroom cloud">mushroom clouds</a> over<br/>
Hiroshima <i>(left)</i> and Nagasaki <i>(right)</i></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2">
<table style="width:100%;margin:0;padding:0;border:0">
<tr>
<th style="padding-right:1em">Date</th>
<td>August 6 and August 9, 1945</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th style="padding-right:1em">Location</th>
<td><span class="location"><a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a></span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th style="padding-right:1em">Result</th>
<td>Allied victory</td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>

<i><a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a></i>:<br/>
<span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a><br/>
<span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="1000" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Canadian_Red_Ensign_%281921-1957%29.svg/23px-Canadian_Red_Ensign_%281921-1957%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Canadian_Red_Ensign_%281921-1957%29.svg/35px-Canadian_Red_Ensign_%281921-1957%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Canadian_Red_Ensign_%281921-1957%29.svg/46px-Canadian_Red_Ensign_%281921-1957%29.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a></td>
<td style="width:50%;padding-left:0.25em"><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="1000" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Flag_of_Japan_%281870-1999%29.svg/22px-Flag_of_Japan_%281870-1999%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Flag_of_Japan_%281870-1999%29.svg/33px-Flag_of_Japan_%281870-1999%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Flag_of_Japan_%281870-1999%29.svg/43px-Flag_of_Japan_%281870-1999%29.svg.png 2x" width="22"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Japan</a></td>

<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="background-color:#B0C4DE;text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;font-size:110%;">Units involved</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width:50%;border-right:1px dotted #aaa;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manhattan_District" title="Manhattan District">Manhattan District</a>: 50 U.S., 2 British<br/>
<a href="/wiki/509th_Composite_Group" title="509th Composite Group">509th Composite Group</a>: 1,770 U.S.</td>
<td style="width:50%;padding-left:0.25em"><a href="/wiki/Second_General_Army_(Japan)" title="Second General Army (Japan)">Second General Army</a>:<br/>
Hiroshima: 40,000 (5 <a href="/wiki/Anti-aircraft_warfare" title="Anti-aircraft warfare">Anti-aircraft batteries</a>)<br/>
Nagasaki: 9,000 (4 Anti-aircraft batteries)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="background-color:#B0C4DE;text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;font-size:110%;">Casualties and losses</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width:50%;border-right:1px dotted #aaa;">20 British, Dutch, and American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war" title="Prisoners of war">prisoners of war</a> killed</td>
<td style="width:50%;padding-left:0.25em">
<p>Hiroshima:</p>
<ul>
<li>20,000 soldiers killed</li>
<li>70,000–126,000 civilians killed</li>
</ul>
<p>Nagasaki:</p>
<ul>
<li>39,000–80,000 killed</li>
</ul>
<b>Total: 129,000–226,000+ killed</b></td>
</tr>
</table>

</ul>

<b><a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Second Sino-Japanese War</a></b></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
<p>During the final stage of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the United States dropped <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a> on the Japanese cities of <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a> on August 6 and 9, 1945, respectively. The United States had dropped the bombs with the consent of the United Kingdom as outlined in the <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Agreement" title="Quebec Agreement">Quebec Agreement</a>. The two bombings killed at least 129,000 people (most of whom were civilians) and remain the only use of nuclear weapons in the history of warfare.</p>
<p>In the final year of the war, the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a> prepared for what was anticipated to be a very costly <a href="/wiki/Operation_Downfall" title="Operation Downfall">invasion of the Japanese mainland</a>. This undertaking was preceded by a U.S. <a href="/wiki/Air_raids_on_Japan" title="Air raids on Japan">conventional and firebombing campaign</a> that destroyed 67 Japanese cities. The <a href="/wiki/European_theatre_of_World_War_II" title="European theatre of World War II">war in Europe</a> had concluded when <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Germany</a> signed its <a href="/wiki/German_Instrument_of_Surrender" title="German Instrument of Surrender">instrument of surrender</a> on May 8, 1945, which was eight days after <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Adolf_Hitler" title="Death of Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler committed suicide</a>. The Japanese were facing the same fate but refused to accept the Allies' demands for <a href="/wiki/Unconditional_surrender" title="Unconditional surrender">unconditional surrender</a>, and the war in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">Pacific Theatre</a> continued. The Allies called for the unconditional surrender of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_armed_forces" title="Imperial Japanese armed forces">Imperial Japanese armed forces</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Declaration" title="Potsdam Declaration">Potsdam Declaration</a> on July 26, 1945—the alternative being "prompt and utter destruction". The Japanese <a href="/wiki/Mokusatsu" title="Mokusatsu">ignored</a> the ultimatum and the war continued.</p>
<p>By August 1945, the Allies' <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> had produced two types of atomic bomb, and the <a href="/wiki/509th_Composite_Group" title="509th Composite Group">509th Composite Group</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Air_Forces" title="United States Army Air Forces">United States Army Air Forces</a> (USAAF) was equipped with the specialized <a href="/wiki/Silverplate" title="Silverplate">Silverplate</a> version of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-29_Superfortress" title="Boeing B-29 Superfortress">Boeing B-29 Superfortress</a> that could deliver them from <a href="/wiki/Tinian" title="Tinian">Tinian</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Islands" title="Mariana Islands">Mariana Islands</a>. Orders for atomic bombs to be used on four Japanese cities were issued on July 25. On August 6, the U.S. dropped a <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> <a href="/wiki/Gun-type_fission_weapon" title="Gun-type fission weapon">gun-type</a> (<a href="/wiki/Little_Boy" title="Little Boy">Little Boy</a>) bomb on Hiroshima, and American President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> called for Japan's surrender, warning it to "expect a rain of ruin from the air, the like of which has never been seen on this earth." Three days later, on August 9, a <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Implosion-type_nuclear_weapon" title="Implosion-type nuclear weapon">implosion-type</a> (<a href="/wiki/Fat_Man" title="Fat Man">Fat Man</a>) bomb was dropped on Nagasaki. Within the first two to four months following the bombings, the acute effects of the atomic bombings had killed 90,000–146,000 people in Hiroshima and 39,000–80,000 in Nagasaki; roughly half of the deaths in each city occurred on the first day. During the following months, large numbers died from the effect of burns, <a href="/wiki/Acute_radiation_syndrome" title="Acute radiation syndrome">radiation sickness</a>, and other injuries, compounded by illness and malnutrition. In both cities, most of the dead were civilians, although Hiroshima had a sizable military garrison.</p>
<p>Japan announced <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">its surrender</a> to the Allies on August 15, six days after the bombing of Nagasaki and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Japanese_War_(1945)" title="Soviet–Japanese War (1945)">declaration of war</a>. On September 2, the Japanese government signed the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Instrument_of_Surrender" title="Japanese Instrument of Surrender">instrument of surrender</a>, effectively <a href="/wiki/End_of_World_War_II_in_Asia" title="End of World War II in Asia">ending World War II</a>. The justification for the bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki is <a href="/wiki/Debate_over_the_atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Debate over the atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">still debated</a> to this day.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pacific_War">Pacific War</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">Pacific War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1945-08-01JapWW2BattlefrontAtlas.jpg"><img alt="A map of East Asia and the Western Pacific during World War II" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1208" data-file-width="1265" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/1945-08-01JapWW2BattlefrontAtlas.jpg/220px-1945-08-01JapWW2BattlefrontAtlas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/1945-08-01JapWW2BattlefrontAtlas.jpg/330px-1945-08-01JapWW2BattlefrontAtlas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/1945-08-01JapWW2BattlefrontAtlas.jpg/440px-1945-08-01JapWW2BattlefrontAtlas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1945-08-01JapWW2BattlefrontAtlas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Situation of the Pacific War by August 1, 1945. Japan still had control of all of <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina</a>, a large part of China, including most of the main Chinese cities, and much of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch East Indies</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1945, the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">Pacific War</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a> entered its fourth year. The Japanese fought fiercely, ensuring that the Allied victory would come at an enormous cost. The 1.25 million battle casualties that were incurred by the United States in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> included both <a href="/wiki/Military_personnel" title="Military personnel">military personnel</a> <a href="/wiki/Killed_in_action" title="Killed in action">killed in action</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wounded_in_action" title="Wounded in action">wounded in action</a>. Nearly one million of the casualties occurred during the last year of the war, from June 1944 to June 1945. In December 1944, American battle casualties hit an all-time monthly high of 88,000 as a result of the German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ardennes_Offensive" title="Ardennes Offensive">Ardennes Offensive</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco20092.E2.80.933.2C_49.E2.80.9351_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco20092.E2.80.933.2C_49.E2.80.9351-1">[1]</a></sup> In the Pacific, the Allies <a href="/wiki/Philippines_Campaign_(1944%E2%80%931945)" title="Philippines Campaign (1944–1945)">returned to the Philippines</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams1960307_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams1960307-2">[2]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma_Campaign_1944%E2%80%931945" title="Burma Campaign 1944–1945">recaptured Burma</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams1960532_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams1960532-3">[3]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Borneo_campaign_(1945)" title="Borneo campaign (1945)">invaded Borneo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams1960527_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams1960527-4">[4]</a></sup> Offensives were undertaken to reduce the Japanese forces remaining in <a href="/wiki/Bougainville_Campaign" title="Bougainville Campaign">Bougainville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aitape%E2%80%93Wewak_campaign" title="Aitape–Wewak campaign">New Guinea</a> and the Philippines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong196348.E2.80.9349_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong196348.E2.80.9349-5">[5]</a></sup> In April 1945, American forces <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Okinawa" title="Battle of Okinawa">landed on Okinawa</a>, where heavy fighting continued until June. Along the way, the ratio of Japanese to American casualties dropped from 5:1 in the Philippines to 2:1 on Okinawa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco20092.E2.80.933.2C_49.E2.80.9351_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco20092.E2.80.933.2C_49.E2.80.9351-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although some Japanese soldiers were taken prisoner, most fought until they were killed or committed <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a>. Nearly 99% of the 21,000 defenders of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Iwo_Jima" title="Battle of Iwo Jima">Iwo Jima</a> were killed. Of the 117,000 Japanese troops defending <a href="/wiki/Okinawa_Island" title="Okinawa Island">Okinawa</a> in April–June 1945, 94% were killed. American military leaders used these figures to estimate high casualties among American soldiers in the planned invasion of Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksStanley200741.E2.80.9344_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksStanley200741.E2.80.9344-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the Allies advanced towards Japan, conditions became steadily worse for the Japanese people. Japan's merchant fleet declined from 5,250,000 <a href="/wiki/Gross_register_tonnage" title="Gross register tonnage">gross tons</a> in 1941 to 1,560,000 tons in March 1945, and 557,000 tons in August 1945. Lack of raw materials forced the Japanese war economy into a steep decline after the middle of 1944. The civilian economy, which had slowly deteriorated throughout the war, reached disastrous levels by the middle of 1945. The loss of shipping also affected the fishing fleet, and the 1945 catch was only 22% of that in 1941. The 1945 rice harvest was the worst since 1909, and hunger and malnutrition became widespread. U.S. industrial production was overwhelmingly superior to Japan's. By 1943, the U.S. produced almost 100,000 aircraft a year, compared to Japan's production of 70,000 for the entire war. By the middle of 1944, the U.S. had almost a hundred aircraft carriers in the Pacific, far more than Japan's twenty-five for the entire war. In February 1945, Prince <a href="/wiki/Fumimaro_Konoe" title="Fumimaro Konoe">Fumimaro Konoe</a> advised the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Hirohito" title="Hirohito">Hirohito</a> that defeat was inevitable, and urged him to abdicate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoox19692540.E2.80.932544_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoox19692540.E2.80.932544-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparations_to_invade_Japan">Preparations to invade Japan</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Operation_Downfall" title="Operation Downfall">Operation Downfall</a></div>
<p>Even before the <a href="/wiki/Victory_in_Europe_Day" title="Victory in Europe Day">surrender of Nazi Germany</a> on May 8, 1945, plans were underway for the largest operation of the Pacific War, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Downfall" title="Operation Downfall">Operation Downfall</a>, the invasion of Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco200932.E2.80.9334_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco200932.E2.80.9334-8">[8]</a></sup> The operation had two parts: <a href="/wiki/Operation_Downfall#Olympic" title="Operation Downfall">Operation Olympic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Operation_Downfall#Coronet" title="Operation Downfall">Operation Coronet</a>. Set to begin in October 1945, Olympic involved a series of landings by the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Sixth_United_States_Army" title="Sixth United States Army">Sixth Army</a> intended to capture the southern third of the southernmost main Japanese island, <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyūshū</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2009125.E2.80.93130_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2009125.E2.80.93130-9">[9]</a></sup> Operation Olympic was to be followed in March 1946 by Operation Coronet, the capture of the <a href="/wiki/Kant%C5%8D_Plain" title="Kantō Plain">Kantō Plain</a>, near Tokyo on the main Japanese island of <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshū</a> by the U.S. <a href="/wiki/First_United_States_Army" title="First United States Army">First</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eighth_United_States_Army" title="Eighth United States Army">Eighth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tenth_United_States_Army" title="Tenth United States Army">Tenth</a> Armies, as well as a <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Corps" title="Commonwealth Corps">Commonwealth Corps</a> made up of Australian, British and Canadian divisions. The target date was chosen to allow for Olympic to complete its objectives, for troops to be redeployed from Europe, and the Japanese winter to pass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2009169.E2.80.93171_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2009169.E2.80.93171-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%22Jap...You%27re_Next%5E_We%27ll_Finish_the_Job%22_-_NARA_-_513563.jpg"><img alt="Uncle Sam holding a spanner, rolling up his sleeves" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2931" data-file-width="2055" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/%22Jap...You%27re_Next%5E_We%27ll_Finish_the_Job%22_-_NARA_-_513563.jpg/170px-%22Jap...You%27re_Next%5E_We%27ll_Finish_the_Job%22_-_NARA_-_513563.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/%22Jap...You%27re_Next%5E_We%27ll_Finish_the_Job%22_-_NARA_-_513563.jpg/255px-%22Jap...You%27re_Next%5E_We%27ll_Finish_the_Job%22_-_NARA_-_513563.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/%22Jap...You%27re_Next%5E_We%27ll_Finish_the_Job%22_-_NARA_-_513563.jpg/340px-%22Jap...You%27re_Next%5E_We%27ll_Finish_the_Job%22_-_NARA_-_513563.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%22Jap...You%27re_Next%5E_We%27ll_Finish_the_Job%22_-_NARA_-_513563.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. Army poster prepares the public for the invasion of Japan after ending war on Germany and Italy</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Japan" title="Geography of Japan">Japan's geography</a> made this invasion plan obvious to the Japanese; they were able to predict the Allied invasion plans accurately and thus adjust their defensive plan, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Ketsug%C5%8D" title="Operation Ketsugō">Operation <i>Ketsugō</i></a>, accordingly. The Japanese planned an all-out defense of Kyūshū, with little left in reserve for any subsequent defense operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco200945.E2.80.9348_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco200945.E2.80.9348-11">[11]</a></sup> Four veteran divisions were withdrawn from the <a href="/wiki/Kwantung_Army" title="Kwantung Army">Kwantung Army</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> in March 1945 to strengthen the forces in Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco200921_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco200921-12">[12]</a></sup> and 45 new divisions were activated between February and May 1945. Most were immobile formations for coastal defense, but 16 were high quality mobile divisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco200970.E2.80.9372_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco200970.E2.80.9372-13">[13]</a></sup> In all, there were 2.3 million <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Army" title="Imperial Japanese Army">Japanese Army</a> troops prepared to defend the home islands, backed by a <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_Fighting_Corps" title="Volunteer Fighting Corps">civilian militia</a> of 28 million men and women. Casualty predictions varied widely, but were extremely high. The Vice Chief of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy_General_Staff" title="Imperial Japanese Navy General Staff">Imperial Japanese Navy General Staff</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vice_Admiral" title="Vice Admiral">Vice Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/Takijir%C5%8D_%C5%8Cnishi" title="Takijirō Ōnishi">Takijirō Ōnishi</a>, predicted up to 20 million Japanese deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2009121.E2.80.93124_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2009121.E2.80.93124-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>A study from June 15, 1945, by the Joint War Plans Committee,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> who provided planning information to the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Joint Chiefs of Staff">Joint Chiefs of Staff</a>, estimated that Olympic would result in between 130,000 and 220,000 U.S. casualties, of which U.S. dead would be in the range from 25,000 to 46,000. Delivered on June 15, 1945, after insight gained from the Battle of Okinawa, the study noted Japan's inadequate defenses due to the very effective sea blockade and the American firebombing campaign. The <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Staff_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Chief of Staff of the United States Army">Chief of Staff of the United States Army</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army" title="General of the Army">General of the Army</a> <a href="/wiki/George_Marshall" title="George Marshall">George Marshall</a>, and the Army Commander in Chief in the Pacific, General of the Army <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a>, signed documents agreeing with the Joint War Plans Committee estimate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarroll200748_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarroll200748-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Americans were alarmed by the Japanese buildup, which was accurately tracked through <a href="/wiki/Ultra" title="Ultra">Ultra</a> intelligence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrea1992202.E2.80.93225_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrea1992202.E2.80.93225-17">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_L._Stimson" title="Henry L. Stimson">Henry L. Stimson</a> was sufficiently concerned about high American estimates of probable casualties to commission his own study by <a href="/wiki/Quincy_Wright" title="Quincy Wright">Quincy Wright</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Shockley" title="William Shockley">William Shockley</a>. Wright and Shockley spoke with Colonels <a href="/wiki/James_McCormack" title="James McCormack">James McCormack</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dean_Rusk" title="Dean Rusk">Dean Rusk</a>, and examined casualty forecasts by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_E._DeBakey" title="Michael E. DeBakey">Michael E. DeBakey</a> and Gilbert Beebe. Wright and Shockley estimated the invading Allies would suffer between 1.7 and 4 million casualties in such a scenario, of whom between 400,000 and 800,000 would be dead, while Japanese fatalities would have been around 5 to 10 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco200998.E2.80.9399_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco200998.E2.80.9399-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrank1999340_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrank1999340-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Marshall began contemplating the use of a weapon that was "readily available and which assuredly can decrease the cost in American lives":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2009112_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2009112-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_warfare" title="Chemical warfare">poison gas</a>. Quantities of <a href="/wiki/Phosgene" title="Phosgene">phosgene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_mustard" title="Sulfur mustard">mustard gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tear_gas" title="Tear gas">tear gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyanogen_chloride" title="Cyanogen chloride">cyanogen chloride</a> were moved to <a href="/wiki/Luzon" title="Luzon">Luzon</a> from stockpiles in Australia and New Guinea in preparation for Operation Olympic, and MacArthur ensured that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_Warfare_Service" title="Chemical Warfare Service">Chemical Warfare Service</a> units were trained in their use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2009112_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco2009112-20">[20]</a></sup> Consideration was also given to using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_weapons" title="Biological weapons">biological weapons</a> against Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffer1985164.E2.80.93165_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffer1985164.E2.80.93165-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_raids_on_Japan">Air raids on Japan</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Air_raids_on_Japan" title="Air raids on Japan">Air raids on Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_B-29A-45-BN_Superfortress_44-61784_6_BG_24_BS_-_Incendiary_Journey.jpg"><img alt="Black and white photo of a four engined World War II-era aircraft being viewed from above while it is flying over a city. A large cloud of smoke is visible immediately below the aircraft." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2244" data-file-width="2657" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Boeing_B-29A-45-BN_Superfortress_44-61784_6_BG_24_BS_-_Incendiary_Journey.jpg/220px-Boeing_B-29A-45-BN_Superfortress_44-61784_6_BG_24_BS_-_Incendiary_Journey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Boeing_B-29A-45-BN_Superfortress_44-61784_6_BG_24_BS_-_Incendiary_Journey.jpg/330px-Boeing_B-29A-45-BN_Superfortress_44-61784_6_BG_24_BS_-_Incendiary_Journey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Boeing_B-29A-45-BN_Superfortress_44-61784_6_BG_24_BS_-_Incendiary_Journey.jpg/440px-Boeing_B-29A-45-BN_Superfortress_44-61784_6_BG_24_BS_-_Incendiary_Journey.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_B-29A-45-BN_Superfortress_44-61784_6_BG_24_BS_-_Incendiary_Journey.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A B-29 over Osaka on June 1, 1945</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While the United States had developed plans for an air campaign against Japan prior to the Pacific War, the capture of Allied bases in the western Pacific in the first weeks of the conflict meant that this offensive did not begin until mid-1944 when the long-ranged <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-29_Superfortress" title="Boeing B-29 Superfortress">Boeing B-29 Superfortress</a> became ready for use in combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate19534_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate19534-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Operation_Matterhorn" title="Operation Matterhorn">Operation Matterhorn</a> involved India-based B-29s staging through bases around <a href="/wiki/Chengdu" title="Chengdu">Chengdu</a> in China to make a series of raids on strategic targets in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate195322.E2.80.9324_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate195322.E2.80.9324-23">[23]</a></sup> This effort failed to achieve the strategic objectives that its planners had intended, largely because of logistical problems, the bomber's mechanical difficulties, the vulnerability of Chinese staging bases, and the extreme range required to reach key Japanese cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953169.E2.80.93175_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953169.E2.80.93175-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Air_Forces" title="United States Army Air Forces">United States Army Air Forces</a> (USAAF) <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_general_(United_States)" title="Brigadier general (United States)">Brigadier General</a> <a href="/wiki/Haywood_S._Hansell" title="Haywood S. Hansell">Haywood S. Hansell</a> determined that <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tinian" title="Tinian">Tinian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Saipan" title="Saipan">Saipan</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Islands" title="Mariana Islands">Mariana Islands</a> would better serve as B-29 bases, but they were in Japanese hands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate195329.E2.80.9331_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate195329.E2.80.9331-25">[25]</a></sup> Strategies were shifted to accommodate the air war,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953507.E2.80.93509_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953507.E2.80.93509-26">[26]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_and_Palau_Islands_campaign" title="Mariana and Palau Islands campaign">islands were captured</a> between June and August 1944. Air bases were developed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953514.E2.80.93521_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953514.E2.80.93521-27">[27]</a></sup> and B-29 operations commenced from the Marianas in October 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953548.E2.80.93551_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953548.E2.80.93551-28">[28]</a></sup> These bases were easily resupplied by cargo ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953536.E2.80.93545_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953536.E2.80.93545-29">[29]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/XXI_Bomber_Command" title="XXI Bomber Command">XXI Bomber Command</a> began missions against Japan on November 18, 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953558.E2.80.93560_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953558.E2.80.93560-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The early attempts to bomb Japan from the Marianas proved just as ineffective as the China-based B-29s had been. Hansell continued the practice of conducting so-called high-altitude <a href="/wiki/Precision_bombing" title="Precision bombing">precision bombing</a>, aimed at key industries and transportation networks, even after these tactics had not produced acceptable results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953566_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953566-31">[31]</a></sup> These efforts proved unsuccessful due to logistical difficulties with the remote location, technical problems with the new and advanced aircraft, unfavorable weather conditions, and enemy action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESandler200124.E2.80.9326_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESandler200124.E2.80.9326-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953574.E2.80.93576_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953574.E2.80.93576-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_1945-3-10-1.jpg"><img alt="A vast devastated area with only a few burned out buildings standing" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="769" data-file-width="1000" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Tokyo_1945-3-10-1.jpg/220px-Tokyo_1945-3-10-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Tokyo_1945-3-10-1.jpg/330px-Tokyo_1945-3-10-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Tokyo_1945-3-10-1.jpg/440px-Tokyo_1945-3-10-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_1945-3-10-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i>Operation Meetinghouse</i> <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Tokyo" title="Bombing of Tokyo">firebombing of Tokyo</a> on the night of March 9–10, 1945, was the single deadliest air raid in history;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deadliest_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-deadliest-34">[34]</a></sup> with a greater area of fire damage and loss of life than either of the nuclear bombings of Hiroshima or Nagasaki.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hansell's successor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_General_(United_States)" title="Major General (United States)">Major General</a> <a href="/wiki/Curtis_LeMay" title="Curtis LeMay">Curtis LeMay</a>, assumed command in January 1945 and initially continued to use the same precision bombing tactics, with equally unsatisfactory results. The attacks initially targeted key industrial facilities but much of the Japanese manufacturing process was carried out in small workshops and private homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953608.E2.80.93610_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953608.E2.80.93610-37">[37]</a></sup> Under pressure from USAAF headquarters in Washington, LeMay changed tactics and decided that low-level <a href="/wiki/Firebombing" title="Firebombing">incendiary raids</a> against Japanese cities were the only way to destroy their production capabilities, shifting from precision bombing to <a href="/wiki/Area_bombardment" title="Area bombardment">area bombardment</a> with incendiaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953568.E2.80.93570_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953568.E2.80.93570-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like most <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II" title="Strategic bombing during World War II">strategic bombing during World War II</a>, the aim of the USAAF offensive against Japan was to destroy the enemy's war industries, kill or disable civilian employees of these industries, and <a href="/wiki/Demoralization_(warfare)" title="Demoralization (warfare)">undermine civilian morale</a>. Civilians who took part in the <a href="/wiki/War_effort" title="War effort">war effort</a> through such activities as building <a href="/wiki/Fortification" title="Fortification">fortifications</a> and manufacturing munitions and other war materials in factories and workshops were considered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combatants" title="Combatants">combatants</a> in a legal sense and therefore liable to be attacked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards199683_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards199683-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWerrell1996250_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWerrell1996250-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Over the next six months, the XXI Bomber Command under LeMay firebombed 67 Japanese cities. The <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Tokyo" title="Bombing of Tokyo">firebombing of Tokyo</a>, codenamed <i>Operation Meetinghouse</i>, on March 9–10 killed an estimated 100,000 people and destroyed 16 square miles (41 km<sup>2</sup>) of the city and 267,000 buildings in a single night. It was the deadliest bombing raid of the war, at a cost of 20 B-29s shot down by flak and fighters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953614.E2.80.93617_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953614.E2.80.93617-41">[41]</a></sup> By May, 75% of bombs dropped were incendiaries designed to burn down Japan's "paper cities". By mid-June, Japan's six largest cities had been devastated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953642.E2.80.93643_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953642.E2.80.93643-42">[42]</a></sup> The end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fighting_on_Okinawa" title="Fighting on Okinawa">fighting on Okinawa</a> that month provided airfields even closer to the Japanese mainland, allowing the bombing campaign to be further escalated. Aircraft flying from Allied <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a> also regularly struck targets in Japan during 1945 in preparation for Operation Downfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKerr1991207_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKerr1991207-43">[43]</a></sup> Firebombing switched to smaller cities, with populations ranging from 60,000 to 350,000. According to <a href="/wiki/Yuki_Tanaka_(historian)" title="Yuki Tanaka (historian)">Yuki Tanaka</a>, the U.S. fire-bombed over a hundred Japanese towns and cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> These raids were also devastating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953653.E2.80.93658_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953653.E2.80.93658-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Japanese military was unable to stop the Allied attacks and the country's <a href="/wiki/Civil_defense" title="Civil defense">civil defense</a> preparations proved inadequate. Japanese fighters and antiaircraft guns had difficulty engaging bombers flying at high altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoox1994412.E2.80.93414_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoox1994412.E2.80.93414-46">[46]</a></sup> From April 1945, the Japanese interceptors also had to face American fighter escorts based on <a href="/wiki/Iwo_Jima" title="Iwo Jima">Iwo Jima</a> and Okinawa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoox1994422_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoox1994422-47">[47]</a></sup> That month, the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Army_Air_Service" title="Imperial Japanese Army Air Service">Imperial Japanese Army Air Service</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy_Air_Service" title="Imperial Japanese Navy Air Service">Imperial Japanese Navy Air Service</a> stopped attempting to intercept the air raids in order to preserve fighter aircraft to counter the expected invasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZalogaNoon201054_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZalogaNoon201054-48">[48]</a></sup> By mid-1945 the Japanese only occasionally scrambled aircraft to intercept individual B-29s conducting reconnaissance sorties over the country, in order to conserve supplies of fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZalogaNoon201058.E2.80.9359_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZalogaNoon201058.E2.80.9359-49">[49]</a></sup> By July 1945, the Japanese had stockpiled 1,156,000 US barrels (137,800,000 l; 36,400,000 US gal; 30,300,000 imp gal) of <a href="/wiki/Avgas" title="Avgas">avgas</a> for the invasion of Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiangreco200979.E2.80.9380_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiangreco200979.E2.80.9380-50">[50]</a></sup> While the Japanese military decided to resume attacks on Allied bombers from late June, by this time there were too few operational fighters available for this change of tactics to hinder the Allied air raids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoox1994429_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoox1994429-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atomic_bomb_development">Atomic bomb development</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a></div>
<p>The discovery of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> by German chemists <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Otto Hahn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Strassmann" title="Fritz Strassmann">Fritz Strassmann</a> in 1938, and its theoretical explanation by <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Lise Meitner</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otto_Frisch" title="Otto Frisch">Otto Frisch</a>, made the development of an atomic bomb a theoretical possibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones19857_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones19857-52">[52]</a></sup> Fears that a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_atomic_bomb_project" title="German atomic bomb project">German atomic bomb project</a> would develop atomic weapons first, especially among scientists who were refugees from Nazi Germany and other fascist countries, were expressed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einstein-Szilard_letter" title="Einstein-Szilard letter">Einstein-Szilard letter</a>. This prompted preliminary research in the United States in late 1939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones198512_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones198512-53">[53]</a></sup> Progress was slow until the arrival of the British <a href="/wiki/MAUD_Committee" title="MAUD Committee">MAUD Committee</a> report in late 1941, which indicated that only 5–10 kilograms of isotopically enriched <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a> was needed for a bomb instead of tons of un-enriched uranium and a neutron moderator (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">heavy water</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGowing196440-43.2C_76-79_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGowing196440-43.2C_76-79-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Working in collaboration with the United Kingdom and Canada, with their respective projects <a href="/wiki/Tube_Alloys" title="Tube Alloys">Tube Alloys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chalk_River_Laboratories" title="Chalk River Laboratories">Chalk River Laboratories</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>, under the direction of Major General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leslie_R._Groves,_Jr." title="Leslie R. Groves, Jr.">Leslie R. Groves, Jr.</a>, of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Corps_of_Engineers" title="United States Army Corps of Engineers">U.S. Army Corps of Engineers</a>, designed and built the first atomic bombs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones198589_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones198589-57">[57]</a></sup> Groves appointed <a href="/wiki/J._Robert_Oppenheimer" title="J. Robert Oppenheimer">J. Robert Oppenheimer</a> to organize and head the project's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos Laboratory">Los Alamos Laboratory</a> in New Mexico, where bomb design work was carried out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones198582-84_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones198582-84-58">[58]</a></sup> Two types of bombs were eventually developed. <a href="/wiki/Little_Boy" title="Little Boy">Little Boy</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Gun-type_fission_weapon" title="Gun-type fission weapon">gun-type fission weapon</a> that used <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a>, a rare <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of uranium separated at the <a href="/wiki/Clinton_Engineer_Works" title="Clinton Engineer Works">Clinton Engineer Works</a> at <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge,_Tennessee" title="Oak Ridge, Tennessee">Oak Ridge, Tennessee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1985522_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1985522-59">[59]</a></sup> The other, known as a <a href="/wiki/Fat_Man" title="Fat Man">Fat Man</a> device (both types named by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Serber" title="Robert Serber">Robert Serber</a>), was a more powerful and efficient, but more complicated, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Implosion-type_nuclear_weapon" title="Implosion-type nuclear weapon">implosion-type nuclear weapon</a> that used <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> created in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> at <a href="/wiki/Hanford_Site" title="Hanford Site">Hanford, Washington</a>. A test implosion weapon, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_gadget" title="The gadget">the gadget</a>, was detonated at <a href="/wiki/Trinity_(nuclear_test)" title="Trinity (nuclear test)">Trinity Site</a>, on July 16, 1945, near <a href="/wiki/Alamogordo,_New_Mexico" title="Alamogordo, New Mexico">Alamogordo, New Mexico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1985511.E2.80.93516_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1985511.E2.80.93516-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>There was a <a href="/wiki/Japanese_nuclear_weapon_program" title="Japanese nuclear weapon program">Japanese nuclear weapon program</a>, but it lacked the human, mineral and financial resources of the Manhattan Project, and never made much progress towards developing an atomic bomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrunden199850.E2.80.9352_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrunden199850.E2.80.9352-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparations">Preparations</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization_and_training">Organization and training</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Agnew_HiroshimaAircraft.jpg"><img alt="Color photo of three silver four engined World War II-era aircraft neatly lined up alongside a runway" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1003" data-file-width="1476" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Agnew_HiroshimaAircraft.jpg/220px-Agnew_HiroshimaAircraft.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Agnew_HiroshimaAircraft.jpg/330px-Agnew_HiroshimaAircraft.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Agnew_HiroshimaAircraft.jpg/440px-Agnew_HiroshimaAircraft.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Agnew_HiroshimaAircraft.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Aircraft of the <a href="/wiki/509th_Composite_Group" title="509th Composite Group">509th Composite Group</a> that took part in the Hiroshima bombing. Left to right: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_Stink_(B-29)" title="Big Stink (B-29)"><i>Big Stink</i></a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Great_Artiste_(B-29)" title="The Great Artiste (B-29)"><i>The Great Artiste</i></a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Enola_Gay" title="Enola Gay">Enola Gay</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/509th_Composite_Group" title="509th Composite Group">509th Composite Group</a> was constituted on December 9, 1944, and activated on December 17, 1944, at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wendover_Army_Air_Field" title="Wendover Army Air Field">Wendover Army Air Field</a>, Utah, commanded by <a href="/wiki/Colonel_(United_States)" title="Colonel (United States)">Colonel</a> <a href="/wiki/Paul_Tibbets" title="Paul Tibbets">Paul Tibbets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-509offhist_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-509offhist-62">[62]</a></sup> Tibbets was assigned to organize and command a <a href="/wiki/Bombardment_group" title="Bombardment group">combat group</a> to develop the means of delivering an atomic weapon against targets in Germany and Japan. Because the flying squadrons of the group consisted of both bomber and transport aircraft, the group was designated as a "composite" rather than a "bombardment" unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Working with the Manhattan Project at Los Alamos, Tibbets selected Wendover for his training base over <a href="/wiki/Great_Bend,_Kansas" title="Great Bend, Kansas">Great Bend, Kansas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mountain_Home,_Idaho" title="Mountain Home, Idaho">Mountain Home, Idaho</a>, because of its remoteness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETibbets1998163.2C_167.E2.80.93168_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETibbets1998163.2C_167.E2.80.93168-64">[64]</a></sup> Each bombardier completed at least 50 practice drops of inert or conventional explosive <a href="/wiki/Pumpkin_bomb" title="Pumpkin bomb">pumpkin bombs</a> and Tibbets declared his group combat-ready.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ready_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ready-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_Tinian_Joint_Chiefs.jpg"><img alt="Three men in military fatigues, without jackets or ties." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="874" data-file-width="1246" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Three_Tinian_Joint_Chiefs.jpg/220px-Three_Tinian_Joint_Chiefs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Three_Tinian_Joint_Chiefs.jpg/330px-Three_Tinian_Joint_Chiefs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Three_Tinian_Joint_Chiefs.jpg/440px-Three_Tinian_Joint_Chiefs.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_Tinian_Joint_Chiefs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The "Tinian Joint Chiefs": Captain William S. Parsons (left), Rear Admiral William R. Purnell (center), and Brigadier General Thomas F. Farrell (right)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 509th Composite Group had an authorized strength of 225 officers and 1,542 enlisted men, almost all of whom eventually deployed to Tinian. In addition to its authorized strength, the 509th had attached to it on Tinian 51 civilian and military personnel from <a href="/wiki/Project_Alberta" title="Project Alberta">Project Alberta</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200525_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200525-66">[66]</a></sup> known as the 1st Technical Detachment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953706_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953706-67">[67]</a></sup> The 509th Composite Group's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/393d_Bombardment_Squadron" title="393d Bombardment Squadron">393d Bombardment Squadron</a> was equipped with 15 <a href="/wiki/Silverplate" title="Silverplate">Silverplate</a> B-29s. These aircraft were specially adapted to carry nuclear weapons, and were equipped with <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection" title="Fuel injection">fuel-injected</a> engines, Curtiss Electric <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reverse_thrust" title="Reverse thrust">reversible-pitch</a> <a href="/wiki/Propeller" title="Propeller">propellers</a>, pneumatic actuators for rapid opening and closing of bomb bay doors and other improvements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200514.E2.80.9315_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200514.E2.80.9315-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ground support echelon of the 509th Composite Group moved by rail on April 26, 1945, to its port of embarkation at <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>, Washington. On May 6 the support elements sailed on the SS <i>Cape Victory</i> for the Marianas, while group materiel was shipped on the SS <i>Emile Berliner</i>. The <i>Cape Victory</i> made brief port calls at <a href="/wiki/Honolulu" title="Honolulu">Honolulu</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eniwetok" title="Eniwetok">Eniwetok</a> but the passengers were not permitted to leave the dock area. An advance party of the air echelon, consisting of 29 officers and 61 enlisted men flew by C-54 to <a href="/wiki/North_Field_(Tinian)" title="North Field (Tinian)">North Field</a> on Tinian, between May 15 and May 22.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>There were also two representatives from Washington, D.C., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brigadier_General_(United_States)" title="Brigadier General (United States)">Brigadier General</a> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Farrell_(general)" title="Thomas Farrell (general)">Thomas Farrell</a>, the deputy commander of the Manhattan Project, and <a href="/wiki/Rear_admiral_(United_States)" title="Rear admiral (United States)">Rear Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/William_R._Purnell" title="William R. Purnell">William R. Purnell</a> of the Military Policy Committee,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2005100_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2005100-70">[70]</a></sup> who were on hand to decide higher policy matters on the spot. Along with Captain <a href="/wiki/William_Sterling_Parsons" title="William Sterling Parsons">William S. Parsons</a>, the commander of Project Alberta, they became known as the "Tinian Joint Chiefs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristman1998176_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristman1998176-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Choice_of_targets">Choice of targets</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atomic_bomb_1945_mission_map.svg"><img alt="map of Japan and the Marianas Islands indicating the routes taken by the raids. One goes straight to Iwo Jima and Hiroshima and back the same way. The other goes to the southern tip of Japan, up to Kokura, down to Nagasaki, and the southwest to Okinawa befofore heading back to Tinian." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2525" data-file-width="1919" height="382" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Atomic_bomb_1945_mission_map.svg/290px-Atomic_bomb_1945_mission_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Atomic_bomb_1945_mission_map.svg/435px-Atomic_bomb_1945_mission_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Atomic_bomb_1945_mission_map.svg/580px-Atomic_bomb_1945_mission_map.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atomic_bomb_1945_mission_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The mission runs of August 6 and 9, with Hiroshima, Nagasaki, and <a href="/wiki/Kokura" title="Kokura">Kokura</a> (the original target for August 9) displayed</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In April 1945, Marshall asked Groves to nominate specific targets for bombing for final approval by himself and Stimson. Groves formed a Target Committee, chaired by himself, that included Farrell, Major John A. Derry, <a href="/wiki/Colonel" title="Colonel">Colonel</a> William P. Fisher, Joyce C. Stearns and <a href="/wiki/David_M._Dennison" title="David M. Dennison">David M. Dennison</a> from the USAAF; and scientists <a href="/wiki/John_von_Neumann" title="John von Neumann">John von Neumann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_R._Wilson" title="Robert R. Wilson">Robert R. Wilson</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Penney,_Baron_Penney" title="William Penney, Baron Penney">William Penney</a> from the Manhattan Project. The Target Committee met in Washington on April 27; at Los Alamos on May 10, where it was able to talk to the scientists and technicians there; and finally in Washington on May 28, where it was briefed by Tibbets and <a href="/wiki/Commander" title="Commander">Commander</a> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Ashworth" title="Frederick Ashworth">Frederick Ashworth</a> from Project Alberta, and the Manhattan Project's scientific advisor, <a href="/wiki/Richard_C._Tolman" title="Richard C. Tolman">Richard C. Tolman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1985528.E2.80.93529_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1985528.E2.80.93529-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Target Committee nominated five targets: <a href="/wiki/Kokura" title="Kokura">Kokura</a>, the site of one of Japan's largest munitions plants; <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a>, an embarkation port and industrial center that was the site of a major military headquarters; <a href="/wiki/Yokohama" title="Yokohama">Yokohama</a>, an urban center for aircraft manufacture, machine tools, docks, electrical equipment and oil refineries; <a href="/wiki/Niigata,_Niigata" title="Niigata, Niigata">Niigata</a>, a port with industrial facilities including steel and aluminum plants and an oil refinery; and <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>, a major industrial center. The target selection was subject to the following criteria:</p>
<ul>
<li>The target was larger than 3 mi (4.8 km) in diameter and was an important target in a large city.</li>
<li>The blast would create effective damage.</li>
<li>The target was unlikely to be attacked by August 1945.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-targetcommittee_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-targetcommittee-73">[73]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>These cities were largely untouched during the nightly bombing raids and the Army Air Forces agreed to leave them off the target list so accurate assessment of the weapon could be made. Hiroshima was described as "an important army depot and port of embarkation in the middle of an urban industrial area. It is a good radar target and it is such a size that a large part of the city could be extensively damaged. There are adjacent hills which are likely to produce a focusing effect which would considerably increase the blast damage. Due to rivers it is not a good <a href="/wiki/Incendiary_device" title="Incendiary device">incendiary</a> target."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-targetcommittee_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-targetcommittee-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Target Committee stated that "It was agreed that psychological factors in the target selection were of great importance. Two aspects of this are (1) obtaining the greatest psychological effect against Japan and (2) making the initial use sufficiently spectacular for the importance of the weapon to be internationally recognized when publicity on it is released. Kyoto had the advantage of being an important center for military industry, as well an intellectual center and hence a population better able to appreciate the significance of the weapon. The <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Imperial_Palace" title="Tokyo Imperial Palace">Emperor's palace</a> in Tokyo has a greater fame than any other target but is of least strategic value."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-targetcommittee_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-targetcommittee-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Edwin_O._Reischauer" title="Edwin O. Reischauer">Edwin O. Reischauer</a>, a Japan expert for the <a href="/wiki/Military_Intelligence_Corps_(United_States_Army)" title="Military Intelligence Corps (United States Army)">U.S. Army Intelligence Service</a>, was incorrectly said to have prevented the bombing of Kyoto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-targetcommittee_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-targetcommittee-73">[73]</a></sup> In his autobiography, Reischauer specifically refuted this claim:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>... the only person deserving credit for saving Kyoto from destruction is Henry L. Stimson, the Secretary of War at the time, who had known and admired Kyoto ever since his honeymoon there several decades earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReischauer1986101_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReischauer1986101-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>On May 30, Stimson asked Groves to remove Kyoto from the target list due to its historical, religious and cultural significance, but Groves pointed to its military and industrial significance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1985529_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1985529-76">[76]</a></sup> Stimson then approached <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> about the matter. Truman agreed with Stimson, and Kyoto was temporarily removed from the target list.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHasegawa200667.E2.80.9368_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHasegawa200667.E2.80.9368-77">[77]</a></sup> Groves attempted to restore Kyoto to the target list in July, but Stimson remained adamant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHasegawa2006149.E2.80.93150_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHasegawa2006149.E2.80.93150-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1985530_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1985530-79">[79]</a></sup> On July 25, <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a> was put on the target list in place of Kyoto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1985530_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1985530-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_demonstration">Proposed demonstration</span></h3>
<p>In early May 1945, the <a href="/wiki/Interim_Committee" title="Interim Committee">Interim Committee</a> was created by Stimson at the urging of leaders of the Manhattan Project and with the approval of Truman to advise on matters pertaining to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_technology" title="Nuclear technology">nuclear energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrank1999255.E2.80.93256_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrank1999255.E2.80.93256-80">[80]</a></sup> During the meetings on May 31 and June 1, scientist <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Lawrence" title="Ernest Lawrence">Ernest Lawrence</a> had suggested giving the Japanese a non-combat demonstration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECompton1956240_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECompton1956240-81">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Compton" title="Arthur Compton">Arthur Compton</a> later recalled that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It was evident that everyone would suspect trickery. If a bomb were exploded in Japan with previous notice, the Japanese air power was still adequate to give serious interference. An atomic bomb was an intricate device, still in the developmental stage. Its operation would be far from routine. If during the final adjustments of the bomb the Japanese defenders should attack, a faulty move might easily result in some kind of failure. Such an end to an advertised demonstration of power would be much worse than if the attempt had not been made. It was now evident that when the time came for the bombs to be used we should have only one of them available, followed afterwards by others at all-too-long intervals. We could not afford the chance that one of them might be a dud. If the test were made on some neutral territory, it was hard to believe that Japan's determined and fanatical military men would be impressed. If such an open test were made first and failed to bring surrender, the chance would be gone to give the shock of surprise that proved so effective. On the contrary, it would make the Japanese ready to interfere with an atomic attack if they could. Though the possibility of a demonstration that would not destroy human lives was attractive, no one could suggest a way in which it could be made so convincing that it would be likely to stop the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECompton1956238.E2.80.93239_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECompton1956238.E2.80.93239-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The possibility of a demonstration was raised again in the <a href="/wiki/Franck_Report" title="Franck Report">Franck Report</a> issued by physicist <a href="/wiki/James_Franck" title="James Franck">James Franck</a> on June 11 and the Scientific Advisory Panel rejected his report on June 16, saying that "we can propose no technical demonstration likely to bring an end to the war; we see no acceptable alternative to direct military use." Franck then took the report to Washington, D.C., where the Interim Committee met on June 21 to re-examine its earlier conclusions; but it reaffirmed that there was no alternative to the use of the bomb on a military target.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrank1999255.E2.80.93260_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrank1999255.E2.80.93260-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like Compton, many U.S. officials and scientists argued that a demonstration would sacrifice the shock value of the atomic attack, and the Japanese could deny the atomic bomb was lethal, making the mission less likely to produce surrender. Allied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war" title="Prisoners of war">prisoners of war</a> might be moved to the demonstration site and be killed by the bomb. They also worried that the bomb might be a dud since the Trinity test was of a stationary device, not an air-dropped bomb. In addition, only two bombs would be available at the start of August, although more were in production, and they cost billions of dollars, so using one for a demonstration would be expensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewman199586_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewman199586-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Generals_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Generals-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaflets">Leaflets</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Firebombing_leaflet.jpg"><img alt="Leaflet showing B-29s dropping bombs. There are 12 circles with 12 Japanese cities named in Japanese writing." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="349" data-file-width="640" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Firebombing_leaflet.jpg/220px-Firebombing_leaflet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Firebombing_leaflet.jpg/330px-Firebombing_leaflet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Firebombing_leaflet.jpg/440px-Firebombing_leaflet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Firebombing_leaflet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Various leaflets were dropped on Japan, three versions showing the names of 11 or 12 Japanese cities targeted for destruction by firebombing. The other side contained text stating "...we cannot promise that only these cities will be among those attacked ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Information_War_in_the_Pacific_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Information_War_in_the_Pacific-86">[86]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For several months, the U.S. had warned civilians of potential air raids by dropping more than 63 million leaflets across Japan. Many Japanese cities suffered terrible damage from aerial bombings; some were as much as 97% destroyed. LeMay thought that leaflets would increase the psychological impact of bombing, and reduce the international stigma of area-bombing cities. Even with the warnings, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_resistance_to_the_Empire_of_Japan_in_World_War_II" title="Japanese resistance to the Empire of Japan in World War II">Japanese opposition to the war</a> remained ineffective. In general, the Japanese regarded the leaflet messages as truthful, with many Japanese choosing to leave major cities. The leaflets caused such concern amongst the Empire of Japan that they ordered the arrest of anyone caught in possession of a leaflet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Information_War_in_the_Pacific_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Information_War_in_the_Pacific-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953656_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953656-87">[87]</a></sup> Leaflet texts were prepared by recent Japanese prisoners of war because they were thought to be the best choice "to appeal to their compatriots".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrank1999153_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrank1999153-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>In preparation for dropping an atomic bomb on Hiroshima, the Oppenheimer-led <a href="/wiki/Interim_Committee" title="Interim Committee">Scientific Panel of the Interim Committee</a> decided against a demonstration bomb and against a special leaflet warning. Those decisions were implemented because of the uncertainty of a successful detonation and also because of the wish to maximize <a href="/wiki/Psychological_warfare" title="Psychological warfare">shock in the leadership</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNelly2000138_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNelly2000138-89">[89]</a></sup> No warning was given to Hiroshima that a new and much more destructive bomb was going to be dropped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELifton199117_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELifton199117-90">[90]</a></sup> Various sources gave conflicting information about when the last leaflets were dropped on Hiroshima prior to the atomic bomb. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Jay_Lifton" title="Robert Jay Lifton">Robert Jay Lifton</a> wrote that it was July 27,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELifton199117_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELifton199117-90">[90]</a></sup> and Theodore H. McNelly wrote that it was July 3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNelly2000138_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNelly2000138-89">[89]</a></sup> The USAAF history noted that eleven cities were targeted with leaflets on July 27, but Hiroshima was not one of them, and there were no leaflet sorties on July 30.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953656_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953656-87">[87]</a></sup> Leaflet sorties were undertaken on August 1 and August 4. Hiroshima may have been leafleted in late July or early August, as survivor accounts talk about a delivery of leaflets a few days before the atomic bomb was dropped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELifton199117_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELifton199117-90">[90]</a></sup> Three versions were printed of a leaflet listing 11 or 12 cities targeted for firebombing; a total of 33 cities listed. With the text of this leaflet reading in Japanese "...we cannot promise that only these cities will be among those attacked..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Information_War_in_the_Pacific_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_Information_War_in_the_Pacific-86">[86]</a></sup> Hiroshima is not listed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungei1981215_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungei1981215-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBradley1999103_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBradley1999103-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller198643_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller198643-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consultation_with_Britain_and_Canada">Consultation with Britain and Canada</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Handy_to_spaatz_1945.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="462" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Handy_to_spaatz_1945.gif/170px-Handy_to_spaatz_1945.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Handy_to_spaatz_1945.gif/255px-Handy_to_spaatz_1945.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Handy_to_spaatz_1945.gif/340px-Handy_to_spaatz_1945.gif 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Handy_to_spaatz_1945.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Handy" title="Thomas Handy">Thomas Handy</a>'s order to General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carl_Spaatz" title="Carl Spaatz">Carl Spaatz</a> authorizing the dropping of the atomic bombs</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under the 1943 <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Agreement" title="Quebec Agreement">Quebec Agreement</a> with the United Kingdom, nuclear weapons would not be used against another country without mutual consent. Stimson therefore had to obtain British permission. A meeting of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combined_Policy_Committee" title="Combined Policy Committee">Combined Policy Committee</a> was held at <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">the Pentagon</a> on July 4, 1945. Stimson and <a href="/wiki/Vannevar_Bush" title="Vannevar Bush">Vannevar Bush</a> represented the United States; Britain was represented by the head of the <a href="/wiki/British_Defence_Staff_%E2%80%93_US" title="British Defence Staff – US">British Joint Staff Mission</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Field_Marshal_(United_Kingdom)" title="Field Marshal (United Kingdom)">Field Marshal</a> Sir <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_Maitland_Wilson,_1st_Baron_Wilson" title="Henry Maitland Wilson, 1st Baron Wilson">Henry Maitland Wilson</a>; and Canada by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clarence_D._Howe" title="Clarence D. Howe">Clarence D. Howe</a>. In addition, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvey_Bundy" title="Harvey Bundy">Harvey Bundy</a>, Sir <a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">James Chadwick</a>, Groves, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Halifax" title="Lord Halifax">Lord Halifax</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_L._Harrison" title="George L. Harrison">George L. Harrison</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roger_Makins" title="Roger Makins">Roger Makins</a> were all present at the meeting by invitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962372.E2.80.93373_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962372.E2.80.93373-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wilson announced that the British government concurred with the use of nuclear weapons against Japan, which would be officially recorded as a decision of the Combined Policy Committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962372.E2.80.93373_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962372.E2.80.93373-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGowing1964372_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGowing1964372-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> As the release of information to third parties was also controlled by the Quebec Agreement, discussion then turned to what scientific details would be revealed in the press announcement of the bombing. The meeting also considered what Truman could reveal to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>, the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, at the upcoming <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Conference" title="Potsdam Conference">Potsdam Conference</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962372.E2.80.93373_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962372.E2.80.93373-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>Orders for the attack were issued to General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carl_Spaatz" title="Carl Spaatz">Carl Spaatz</a> on July 25 under the signature of General <a href="/wiki/Thomas_T._Handy" title="Thomas T. Handy">Thomas T. Handy</a>, the acting Chief of Staff, since Marshall was at the Potsdam Conference with Truman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953712.E2.80.93713_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953712.E2.80.93713-98">[98]</a></sup> That day, Truman noted in his diary that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>This weapon is to be used against Japan between now and August 10th. I have told the Sec. of War, Mr. Stimson, to use it so that military objectives and soldiers and sailors are the target and not women and children. Even if the Japs are savages, ruthless, merciless and fanatic, we as the leader of the world for the common welfare cannot drop that terrible bomb on the old capital [Kyoto] or the new [Tokyo]. He and I are in accord. The target will be a purely military one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Potsdam_Declaration">Potsdam Declaration</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Declaration" title="Potsdam Declaration">Potsdam Declaration</a></div>
<p>The July 16 success of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinity_Test" title="Trinity Test">Trinity Test</a> in the <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> desert exceeded expectations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962389.E2.80.93390_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962389.E2.80.93390-100">[100]</a></sup> On July 26, Allied leaders issued the <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Declaration" title="Potsdam Declaration">Potsdam Declaration</a>, which outlined the terms of surrender for Japan. The declaration was presented as an <a href="/wiki/Ultimatum" title="Ultimatum">ultimatum</a> and stated that without a surrender, the Allies would attack Japan, resulting in "the inevitable and complete destruction of the Japanese armed forces and just as inevitably the utter devastation of the Japanese homeland". The atomic bomb was not mentioned in the communiqué.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962395.E2.80.93396_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewlettAnderson1962395.E2.80.93396-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 28, Japanese papers reported that the declaration had been rejected by the Japanese government.
