<td>Strike spare—did not complete mission</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>At 03:49 on the morning of August 9, 1945, <i>Bockscar</i>, flown by Sweeney's crew, carried Fat Man, with Kokura as the primary target and Nagasaki the secondary target. The mission plan for the second attack was nearly identical to that of the Hiroshima mission, with two B-29s flying an hour ahead as weather scouts and two additional B-29s in Sweeney's flight for instrumentation and photographic support of the mission. Sweeney took off with his weapon already armed but with the electrical safety plugs still engaged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SM16_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-SM16-196">[196]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Agnew_StrikeOrderNagasaki.jpg"><img alt="A page of typed instructions" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="617" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Agnew_StrikeOrderNagasaki.jpg/170px-Agnew_StrikeOrderNagasaki.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Agnew_StrikeOrderNagasaki.jpg/255px-Agnew_StrikeOrderNagasaki.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Agnew_StrikeOrderNagasaki.jpg/340px-Agnew_StrikeOrderNagasaki.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Agnew_StrikeOrderNagasaki.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Strike order for the Nagasaki bombing as posted August 8, 1945</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During pre-flight inspection of <i>Bockscar</i>, the flight engineer notified Sweeney that an inoperative fuel transfer pump made it impossible to use 640 US gallons (2,400 l; 530 imp gal) of fuel carried in a reserve tank. This fuel would still have to be carried all the way to Japan and back, consuming still more fuel. Replacing the pump would take hours; moving the Fat Man to another aircraft might take just as long and was dangerous as well, as the bomb was live. Tibbets and Sweeney therefore elected to have <i>Bockscar</i> continue the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESweeneyAntonucciAntonucci1997204.E2.80.93205_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESweeneyAntonucciAntonucci1997204.E2.80.93205-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Story_of_Nagasaki_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Story_of_Nagasaki-198">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>This time Penney and Cheshire were allowed to accompany the mission, flying as observers on the third plane, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_Stink_(B-29)" title="Big Stink (B-29)">Big Stink</a></i>, flown by the group's operations officer, Major James I. Hopkins, Jr. Observers aboard the weather planes reported both targets clear. When Sweeney's aircraft arrived at the assembly point for his flight off the coast of Japan, <i>Big Stink</i> failed to make the rendezvous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SM16_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-SM16-196">[196]</a></sup> According to Cheshire, Hopkins was at varying heights including 9,000 feet (2,700 m) higher than he should have been, and was not flying tight circles over <a href="/wiki/Yakushima" title="Yakushima">Yakushima</a> as previously agreed with Sweeney and Captain <a href="/wiki/Frederick_C._Bock" title="Frederick C. Bock">Frederick C. Bock</a>, who was piloting the support B-29 <i>The Great Artiste</i>. Instead, Hopkins was flying 40-mile (64 km) dogleg patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESweeneyAntonucciAntonucci1997212_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESweeneyAntonucciAntonucci1997212-199">[199]</a></sup> Though ordered not to circle longer than fifteen minutes, Sweeney continued to wait for <i>Big Stink</i>, at the urging of Ashworth, the plane's weaponeer, who was in command of the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953720_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECravenCate1953720-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>After exceeding the original departure time limit by a half-hour, <i>Bockscar</i>, accompanied by <i>The Great Artiste</i>, proceeded to Kokura, thirty minutes away. The delay at the rendezvous had resulted in clouds and drifting smoke over Kokura from fires started by a major firebombing raid by 224 B-29s on nearby <a href="/wiki/Yahata,_Fukuoka" title="Yahata, Fukuoka">Yahata</a> the previous day. Additionally, the Yawata Steel Works intentionally burned <a href="/wiki/Coal_tar" title="Coal tar">coal tar</a>, to produce black smoke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> The clouds and smoke resulted in 70% of the area over Kokura being covered, obscuring the aiming point. Three bomb runs were made over the next 50 minutes, burning fuel and exposing the aircraft repeatedly to the heavy defenses of Yawata, but the bombardier was unable to drop visually. By the time of the third bomb run, Japanese antiaircraft fire was getting close, and Second Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Beser" title="Jacob Beser">Jacob Beser</a>, who was monitoring Japanese communications, reported activity on the Japanese fighter direction radio bands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESweeneyAntonucciAntonucci1997179.2C_213.E2.80.93215_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESweeneyAntonucciAntonucci1997179.2C_213.E2.80.93215-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<p>After three runs over the city, and with fuel running low because of the failed fuel pump, they headed for their secondary target, Nagasaki.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SM16_196-2"><a href="#cite_note-SM16-196">[196]</a></sup> Fuel consumption calculations made en route indicated that <i>Bockscar</i> had insufficient fuel to reach Iwo Jima and would be forced to divert to <a href="/wiki/Okinawa_Prefecture" title="Okinawa Prefecture">Okinawa</a>, which had become entirely Allied-occupied territory <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Okinawa#Southern_Okinawa" title="Battle of Okinawa">only six weeks earlier</a>. After initially deciding that if Nagasaki were obscured on their arrival the crew would carry the bomb to Okinawa and dispose of it in the ocean if necessary, Ashworth ruled that a radar approach would be used if the target was obscured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spitzer_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spitzer-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<p>At about 07:50 Japanese time, an air raid alert was sounded in Nagasaki, but the "all clear" signal was given at 08:30. When only two B-29 Superfortresses were sighted at 10:53, the Japanese apparently assumed that the planes were only on reconnaissance and no further alarm was given.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChun200870_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChun200870-204">[204]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nagasaki_1945_-_Before_and_after_(adjusted).jpg"><img alt="The before image looks like a city. In the after image, everything has been obliterated and it is recognisable as the same area only by the rivers running through it, which form an island in the centre of the photographs." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1095" data-file-width="1000" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Nagasaki_1945_-_Before_and_after_%28adjusted%29.jpg/220px-Nagasaki_1945_-_Before_and_after_%28adjusted%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Nagasaki_1945_-_Before_and_after_%28adjusted%29.jpg/330px-Nagasaki_1945_-_Before_and_after_%28adjusted%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Nagasaki_1945_-_Before_and_after_%28adjusted%29.jpg/440px-Nagasaki_1945_-_Before_and_after_%28adjusted%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nagasaki_1945_-_Before_and_after_(adjusted).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nagasaki before and after the bombing and the fires had long since burnt out</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A few minutes later at 11:00, <i>The Great Artiste</i> dropped instruments attached to three parachutes. These instruments also contained an unsigned letter to Professor Ryokichi Sagane, a physicist at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tokyo" title="University of Tokyo">University of Tokyo</a> who studied with three of the scientists responsible for the atomic bomb at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a>, urging him to tell the public about the danger involved with these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="Weapons of mass destruction">weapons of mass destruction</a>. The messages were found by military authorities but not turned over to Sagane until a month later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoddesonHenriksenMeadeWestfall1993295_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoddesonHenriksenMeadeWestfall1993295-205">[205]</a></sup> In 1949, one of the authors of the letter, <a href="/wiki/Luis_Walter_Alvarez" title="Luis Walter Alvarez">Luis Alvarez</a>, met with Sagane and signed the document.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stories_from_Riken_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stories_from_Riken-206">[206]</a></sup></p>
<p>At 11:01, a last-minute break in the clouds over Nagasaki allowed <i>Bockscar</i><span style="padding-left:0.2em;">'</span>s bombardier, Captain <a href="/wiki/Kermit_Beahan" title="Kermit Beahan">Kermit Beahan</a>, to visually sight the target as ordered. The Fat Man weapon, containing a core of about 6.4 kg (14 lb) of <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>, was dropped over the city's industrial valley at <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki&amp;params=32.77372_N_129.86325_E_"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">32°46′25″N</span> <span class="longitude">129°51′48″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">32.77372°N 129.86325°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">32.77372; 129.86325</span></span></span></a></span>. It exploded 47 seconds later at 1,650 ± 33 ft (503 ± 10 m), above a tennis court<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKerr_et_al200543.2C_46_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKerr_et_al200543.2C_46-207">[207]</a></sup> halfway between the Mitsubishi Steel and Arms Works in the south and the Nagasaki Arsenal in the north. This was nearly 3 km (1.9 mi) northwest of the planned hypocenter; the blast was confined to the <a href="/wiki/Urakami" title="Urakami">Urakami Valley</a> and a major portion of the city was protected by the intervening hills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWainstock199692_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWainstock199692-208">[208]</a></sup> The resulting explosion released the equivalent energy of 21 ± 2 kt (87.9 ± 8.4 TJ).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKerr_et_al200542.E2.80.9343_136-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKerr_et_al200542.E2.80.9343-136">[136]</a></sup> The explosion generated temperatures inside the fireball estimated at 3,900 °C (7,050 °F)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2017)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> and winds that were estimated at over 1,000 km/h (620 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDelgado2009107_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDelgado2009107-210">[210]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Naming_conventions_(geographic_names)" title="Wikipedia:Naming conventions (geographic names)"><span title="The geographic scope near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2017)">where?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><i>Big Stink</i> spotted the explosion from a hundred miles away, and flew over to observe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroves1962346_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroves1962346-211">[211]</a></sup> Because of the delays in the mission and the inoperative fuel transfer pump, <i>Bockscar</i> did not have sufficient fuel to reach the emergency landing field at Iwo Jima, so Sweeney and Bock flew to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Okinawa" title="Okinawa">Okinawa</a>. Arriving there, Sweeney circled for 20 minutes trying to contact the control tower for landing clearance, finally concluding that his radio was faulty. Critically low on fuel, <i>Bockscar</i> barely made it to the runway on Okinawa's <a href="/wiki/Yontan_Airfield" title="Yontan Airfield">Yontan Airfield</a>. With enough fuel for only one landing attempt, Sweeney and Albury brought <i>Bockscar</i> in at 150 miles per hour (240 km/h) instead of the normal 120 miles per hour (190 km/h), firing distress flares to alert the field of the uncleared landing. The number two engine died from fuel starvation as <i>Bockscar</i> began its final approach. Touching the runway hard, the heavy B-29 slewed left and towards a row of parked B-24 bombers before the pilots managed to regain control. The B-29's reversible propellers were insufficient to slow the aircraft adequately, and with both pilots standing on the brakes, <i>Bockscar</i> made a swerving 90-degree turn at the end of the runway to avoid running off it. A second engine died from fuel exhaustion by the time the plane came to a stop. The flight engineer later measured fuel in the tanks and concluded that less than five minutes total remained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESweeneyAntonucciAntonucci1997222.E2.80.93226_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESweeneyAntonucciAntonucci1997222.E2.80.93226-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the mission, there was confusion over the identification of the plane. The first eyewitness account by war correspondent <a href="/wiki/William_L._Laurence" title="William L. Laurence">William L. Laurence</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, who accompanied the mission aboard the aircraft piloted by Bock, reported that Sweeney was leading the mission in <i>The Great Artiste</i>. He also noted its "Victor" number as 77, which was that of <i>Bockscar</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eyewitness_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eyewitness-213">[213]</a></sup> Laurence had interviewed Sweeney and his crew, and was aware that they referred to their airplane as <i>The Great Artiste</i>. Except for <i>Enola Gay</i>, none of the 393d's B-29s had yet had names painted on the noses, a fact which Laurence himself noted in his account. Unaware of the switch in aircraft, Laurence assumed Victor 77 was <i>The Great Artiste</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2005222_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2005222-214">[214]</a></sup> which was in fact, Victor 89.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2005184_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2005184-215">[215]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="Events_on_the_ground_Nagasaki"></span></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Events_on_the_ground_2">Events on the ground</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sumiteru_Taniguchi_back.jpg"><img alt="A boy lying down. His back is bloody." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="500" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Sumiteru_Taniguchi_back.jpg/220px-Sumiteru_Taniguchi_back.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Sumiteru_Taniguchi_back.jpg/330px-Sumiteru_Taniguchi_back.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Sumiteru_Taniguchi_back.jpg/440px-Sumiteru_Taniguchi_back.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sumiteru_Taniguchi_back.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A photograph of <a href="/wiki/Sumiteru_Taniguchi" title="Sumiteru Taniguchi">Sumiteru Taniguchi</a>'s back injuries taken in January 1946 by a U.S. Marine photographer</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although the bomb was more powerful than the one used on Hiroshima, the effect was confined by hillsides to the narrow Urakami Valley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHam2011367_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHam2011367-216">[216]</a></sup> Of 7,500 Japanese employees who worked inside the Mitsubishi Munitions plant, including "mobilized"/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conscripted" title="Conscripted">conscripted</a> students and regular workers, 6,200 were killed. Some 17,000–22,000 others who worked in other war plants and factories in the city died as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESklar198456.E2.80.9360_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESklar198456.E2.80.9360-217">[217]</a></sup> Casualty estimates for immediate deaths vary widely, ranging from 22,000 to 75,000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESklar198456.E2.80.9360_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESklar198456.E2.80.9360-217">[217]</a></sup> At least 35,000–40,000 people were killed and 60,000 others injured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESodei1998ix_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESodei1998ix-219">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOE-NAGA_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-DOE-NAGA-220">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNA-221">[221]</a></sup> In the days and months following the explosion, more people died from bomb effects. Because of the presence of undocumented foreign workers, and a number of military personnel in transit, there are great discrepancies in the estimates of total deaths by the end of 1945; a range of 39,000 to 80,000 can be found in various studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rerf-deaths_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-rerf-deaths-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-WNA-221">[221]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UrakamiTenshudoJan1946.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="569" data-file-width="840" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/UrakamiTenshudoJan1946.jpg/220px-UrakamiTenshudoJan1946.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/UrakamiTenshudoJan1946.jpg/330px-UrakamiTenshudoJan1946.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/UrakamiTenshudoJan1946.jpg/440px-UrakamiTenshudoJan1946.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UrakamiTenshudoJan1946.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Urakami" title="Urakami">Urakami</a> Tenshudo (Catholic Church in Nagasaki) destroyed by the bomb, the dome/bell of the church, at right, having toppled off</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Unlike Hiroshima's military death toll, only 150 Japanese soldiers were killed instantly, including thirty-six from the 134th AAA Regiment of the 4th AAA Division.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZalogaNoon201059_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZalogaNoon201059-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlperovitzTree1996534_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlperovitzTree1996534-222">[222]</a></sup> At least eight known <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_war" title="Prisoner of war">POWs</a> died from the bombing and as many as 13 may have died, including a British prisoner of war, Royal Air Force Corporal <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Shaw" title="Ronald Shaw">Ronald Shaw</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> and seven Dutch POWs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> One American POW, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Kieyoomia" title="Joe Kieyoomia">Joe Kieyoomia</a>, was in Nagasaki at the time of the bombing but survived, reportedly having been shielded from the effects of the bomb by the concrete walls of his cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> There were 24 Australian POWs in Nagasaki, all of whom survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup></p>
<p>The radius of total destruction was about 1 mi (1.6 km), followed by fires across the northern portion of the city to 2 mi (3.2 km) south of the bomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-web.archive.org_DNATR805512F_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-web.archive.org_DNATR805512F-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pd_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-pd-227">[227]</a></sup> About 58% of the Mitsubishi Arms Plant was damaged, and about 78% of the Mitsubishi Steel Works. The Mitsubishi Electric Works suffered only 10% structural damage as it was on the border of the main destruction zone. The Nagasaki Arsenal was destroyed in the blast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although many fires likewise burnt following the bombing, in contrast to Hiroshima where sufficient <a href="/wiki/Firestorm" title="Firestorm">fuel density</a> was available, no <a href="/wiki/Firestorm" title="Firestorm">firestorm</a> developed in Nagasaki as the damaged areas did not furnish enough fuel to generate the phenomenon. Instead, the ambient wind at the time pushed the fire spread along the valley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlasstoneDolan1977304_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlasstoneDolan1977304-229">[229]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plans_for_more_atomic_attacks_on_Japan">Plans for more atomic attacks on Japan</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nagasaki_temple_destroyed.jpg"><img alt="A pile of rubble surmounted by a statue of Buddha" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2278" data-file-width="2778" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Nagasaki_temple_destroyed.jpg/220px-Nagasaki_temple_destroyed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Nagasaki_temple_destroyed.jpg/330px-Nagasaki_temple_destroyed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Nagasaki_temple_destroyed.jpg/440px-Nagasaki_temple_destroyed.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nagasaki_temple_destroyed.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Nagasaki Prefecture Report on the bombing characterized Nagasaki as "like a graveyard with not a tombstone standing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Groves expected to have another atomic bomb ready for use on August 19, with three more in September and a further three in October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Generals_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Generals-85">[85]</a></sup> On August 10, he sent a memorandum to Marshall in which he wrote that "the next bomb ... should be ready for delivery on the first suitable weather after 17 or 18 August." On the same day, Marshall endorsed the memo with the comment, "It is not to be released over Japan without express authority from the President."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Generals_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-Generals-85">[85]</a></sup> Truman had secretly requested this on August 10. This modified the previous order that the target cities were to be attacked with atomic bombs "as made ready".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eclipsed_by_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eclipsed_by_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki-231">[231]</a></sup></p>
<p>There was already discussion in the War Department about conserving the bombs then in production for <a href="/wiki/Operation_Downfall" title="Operation Downfall">Operation Downfall</a>. "The problem now [August 13] is whether or not, assuming the Japanese do not capitulate, to continue dropping them every time one is made and shipped out there or whether to hold them ... and then pour them all on in a reasonably short time. Not all in one day, but over a short period. And that also takes into consideration the target that we are after. In other words, should we not concentrate on targets that will be of the greatest assistance to an invasion rather than industry, morale, psychology, and the like? Nearer the tactical use rather than other use."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Generals_85-3"><a href="#cite_note-Generals-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two more Fat Man assemblies were readied, and scheduled to leave <a href="/wiki/Kirtland_Air_Force_Base" title="Kirtland Air Force Base">Kirtland Field</a> for Tinian on August 11 and 14,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoddesonHenriksenMeadeWestfall1993396.E2.80.93397_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoddesonHenriksenMeadeWestfall1993396.E2.80.93397-232">[232]</a></sup> and Tibbets was ordered by LeMay to return to <a href="/wiki/Albuquerque,_New_Mexico" title="Albuquerque, New Mexico">Albuquerque, New Mexico</a>, to collect them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PaulTibbets_StudsTerkel_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-PaulTibbets_StudsTerkel-233">[233]</a></sup> At Los Alamos, technicians worked 24 hours straight to cast <a href="/wiki/Demon_core" title="Demon core">another plutonium core</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> Although cast, it still needed to be pressed and coated, which would take until August 16.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> Therefore, it could have been ready for use on August 19. Unable to reach Marshall, Groves ordered on his own authority on August 13 that the core should not be shipped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eclipsed_by_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki_231-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eclipsed_by_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki-231">[231]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Surrender_of_Japan_and_subsequent_occupation">Surrender of Japan and subsequent occupation</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">Surrender of Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Japan" title="Occupation of Japan">Occupation of Japan</a></div>
<p>Until August 9, Japan's war council still insisted on its four conditions for surrender. On that day Hirohito ordered <a href="/wiki/K%C5%8Dichi_Kido" title="Kōichi Kido">Kōichi Kido</a> to "quickly control the situation ... because the Soviet Union has declared war against us." He then held an Imperial conference during which he authorized minister <a href="/wiki/Shigenori_T%C5%8Dg%C5%8D" title="Shigenori Tōgō">Shigenori Tōgō</a> to notify the Allies that Japan would accept their terms on one condition, that the declaration "does not comprise any demand which prejudices the prerogatives of His Majesty as a Sovereign ruler."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKidoYoshitake19661223_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKidoYoshitake19661223-236">[236]</a></sup></p>
<p>On August 12, the Emperor informed the imperial family of his decision to surrender. One of his uncles, <a href="/wiki/Prince_Yasuhiko_Asaka" title="Prince Yasuhiko Asaka">Prince Asaka</a>, then asked whether the war would be continued if the <i>kokutai</i> could not be preserved. Hirohito simply replied, "Of course."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFujiwara1991129_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFujiwara1991129-237">[237]</a></sup> As the Allied terms seemed to leave intact the principle of the preservation of the Throne, Hirohito recorded on August 14 his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gyokuon-h%C5%8Ds%C5%8D" title="Gyokuon-hōsō">capitulation announcement</a> which was broadcast to the Japanese nation the next day despite a short <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ky%C5%ABj%C5%8D_Incident" title="Kyūjō Incident">rebellion</a> by militarists opposed to the surrender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrank1999316.E2.80.93319_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrank1999316.E2.80.93319-238">[238]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his declaration, Hirohito referred to the atomic bombings:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Moreover, the enemy now possesses a new and terrible weapon with the power to destroy many innocent lives and do incalculable damage. Should we continue to fight, not only would it result in an ultimate collapse and obliteration of the Japanese nation, but also it would lead to the total extinction of human civilization.</p>
<p>Such being the case, how are we to save the millions of our subjects, or to atone ourselves before the hallowed spirits of our imperial ancestors? This is the reason why we have ordered the acceptance of the provisions of the joint declaration of the powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In his "Rescript to the Soldiers and Sailors" delivered on August 17, he stressed the impact of the Soviet invasion on his decision to surrender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> Hirohito met with General MacArthur on September 27, saying to him that "[t]he peace party did not prevail until the bombing of Hiroshima created a situation which could be dramatized". Furthermore, the "Rescript to the Soldiers and Sailors" speech he told MacArthur about was just personal, not political, and never stated that the Soviet intervention in Manchuria was the main reason for surrender. In fact, a day after the bombing of Nagasaki and the Soviet invasion of Manchuria, Hirohito ordered his advisers, primarily <a href="/wiki/Chief_Cabinet_Secretary" title="Chief Cabinet Secretary">Chief Cabinet Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Hisatsune_Sakomizu" title="Hisatsune Sakomizu">Hisatsune Sakomizu</a>, Kawada Mizuho, and <a href="/wiki/Masahiro_Yasuoka" title="Masahiro Yasuoka">Masahiro Yasuoka</a>, to write up a surrender speech. In Hirohito's speech, days before announcing it on radio on August 15, he gave three major reasons for surrender: Tokyo's defenses would not be complete before the American invasion of Japan, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ise_Shrine" title="Ise Shrine">Ise Shrine</a> would be lost to the Americans, and atomic weapons deployed by the Americans would lead to the death of the entire Japanese race. Despite the Soviet intervention, Hirohito did not mention the Soviets as the main factor for surrender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey2007234.E2.80.93240_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey2007234.E2.80.93240-241">[241]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Depiction,_public_response,_and_censorship"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Depiction.2C_public_response.2C_and_censorship">Depiction, public response, and censorship</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_1" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:480px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_2" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Hiroshima_Aftermath_1946_USAF_Film.ogv/480px--Hiroshima_Aftermath_1946_USAF_Film.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:480px;height:360px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="1036.5910204082" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Hiroshima_Aftermath_1946_USAF_Film.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6d/Hiroshima_Aftermath_1946_USAF_Film.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="320" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="250224" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'><img alt="File:Hiroshima Aftermath 1946 USAF Film.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Hiroshima_Aftermath_1946_USAF_Film.ogv/220px-seek%3D330-Hiroshima_Aftermath_1946_USAF_Film.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:165px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6d/Hiroshima_Aftermath_1946_USAF_Film.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hiroshima_Aftermath_1946_USAF_Film.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Life among the rubble in Hiroshima in March and April 1946. Film footage taken by Lieutenant Daniel A. McGovern (director) and Harry Mimura (cameraman) for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing_survey" title="Strategic bombing survey">United States Strategic Bombing Survey</a> project.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the war, the British embassy in Washington reported that Americans regarded the Japanese as "a nameless mass of vermin";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> caricatures depicting Japanese as less than human, e.g. monkeys, were common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> A 1944 opinion poll that asked what should be done with Japan found that 13% of the U.S. public were in favor of "killing off" all Japanese people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBagby1999135_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBagby1999135-244">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeraru1950101_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeraru1950101-245">[245]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the Hiroshima bomb detonated successfully, Oppenheimer addressed an assembly at Los Alamos "clasping his hands together like a prize-winning boxer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk2012450_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk2012450-246">[246]</a></sup> The bombing amazed Otto Hahn and other German atomic scientists the British held at Farm Hall in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Epsilon" title="Operation Epsilon">Operation Epsilon</a>. Hahn stated that he had not believed an atomic weapon "would be possible for another twenty years"; <a href="/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg" title="Werner Heisenberg">Werner Heisenberg</a> did not believe the news at first. <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_von_Weizs%C3%A4cker" title="Carl Friedrich von Weizsäcker">Carl Friedrich von Weizsäcker</a> said "I think it's dreadful of the Americans to have done it. I think it is madness on their part", but Heisenberg replied, "One could equally well say 'That's the quickest way of ending the war'". Hahn was grateful that the German project had not succeeded in developing "such an inhumane weapon"; <a href="/wiki/Karl_Wirtz" title="Karl Wirtz">Karl Wirtz</a> observed that even if it had, "we would have obliterated London but would still not have conquered the world, and then they would have dropped them on us".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farmhall_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-farmhall-247">[247]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hahn told the others, "Once I wanted to suggest that all uranium should be sunk to the bottom of the ocean".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farmhall_247-1"><a href="#cite_note-farmhall-247">[247]</a></sup> The Vatican agreed; <i><a href="/wiki/L%27Osservatore_Romano" title="L'Osservatore Romano">L'Osservatore Romano</a></i> expressed regret that the bomb's inventors did not destroy the weapon for the benefit of humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap19450807_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-ap19450807-248">[248]</a></sup> Rev. <a href="/wiki/Cuthbert_Thicknesse" title="Cuthbert Thicknesse">Cuthbert Thicknesse</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dean_of_St_Albans" title="Dean of St Albans">Dean of St Albans</a>, prohibited using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Albans_Abbey" title="St Albans Abbey">St Albans Abbey</a> for a thanksgiving service for the war's end, calling the use of atomic weapons "an act of wholesale, indiscriminate massacre".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian19450815_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian19450815-249">[249]</a></sup> Nonetheless, news of the atomic bombing was greeted enthusiastically in the U.S.; a poll in <a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)"><i>Fortune</i> magazine</a> in late 1945 showed a significant minority of Americans (22.7%) wishing that more atomic bombs could have been dropped on Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHixson2002239_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHixson2002239-250">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite19951.E2.80.938_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite19951.E2.80.938-251">[251]</a></sup> The initial positive response was supported by the imagery presented to the public (mainly the powerful images of the <a href="/wiki/Mushroom_cloud" title="Mushroom cloud">mushroom cloud</a>) and the censorship of photographs that showed corpses and maimed survivors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHixson2002239_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHixson2002239-250">[250]</a></sup> Such "censorship" was however the status-quo at the time, with no major news outlets depicting corpses or maimed survivors as a result from other events, US or otherwise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeinSelden199725.E2.80.9326_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeinSelden199725.E2.80.9326-252">[252]</a></sup></p>
<p>On August 10, 1945, the day after the Nagasaki bombing, <a href="/wiki/Y%C5%8Dsuke_Yamahata" title="Yōsuke Yamahata">Yōsuke Yamahata</a>, correspondent Higashi and artist Yamada arrived in the city with orders to record the destruction for maximum <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a> purposes, Yamahata took scores of photographs and on August 21 they appeared in <a href="/wiki/Mainichi_Shimbun" title="Mainichi Shimbun">Mainichi Shimbun</a>, a popular Japanese newspaper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wilfred_Burchett" title="Wilfred Burchett">Wilfred Burchett</a> was the first western journalist to visit Hiroshima after the atom bomb was dropped, arriving alone by train from Tokyo on September 2, the day of the formal surrender aboard the <a href="/wiki/USS_Missouri_(BB-63)" title="USS Missouri (BB-63)">USS <i>Missouri</i></a>. His <a href="/wiki/Morse_code" title="Morse code">Morse code</a> dispatch was printed by the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Express" title="Daily Express">Daily Express</a></i> newspaper in London on September 5, 1945, entitled "The Atomic Plague", the first public report to mention the effects of <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fallout" title="Nuclear fallout">nuclear fallout</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurchett200410.E2.80.9325_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurchett200410.E2.80.9325-254">[254]</a></sup> Burchett's reporting was unpopular with the U.S. military. The U.S. censors suppressed a supporting story submitted by <a href="/wiki/George_Weller" title="George Weller">George Weller</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_Daily_News" title="Chicago Daily News">Chicago Daily News</a></i>, and accused Burchett of being under the sway of Japanese propaganda. Laurence dismissed the reports on radiation sickness as Japanese efforts to undermine American morale, ignoring his own account of Hiroshima's radiation sickness published one week earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_3" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:400px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_4" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Physical_damage%2C_blast_effect%2C_Hiroshima%2C_1946-03-13_~_1946-04-08%2C_342-USAF-11071.ogv/400px--Physical_damage%2C_blast_effect%2C_Hiroshima%2C_1946-03-13_~_1946-04-08%2C_342-USAF-11071.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:400px;height:300px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="1266.9990666667" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Physical_damage,_blast_effect,_Hiroshima,_1946-03-13_~_1946-04-08,_342-USAF-11071.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/df/Physical_damage%2C_blast_effect%2C_Hiroshima%2C_1946-03-13_~_1946-04-08%2C_342-USAF-11071.ogv" type="video/ogg; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Physical_damage,_blast_effect,_Hiroshima,_1946-03-13_~_1946-04-08,_342-USAF-11071.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Hiroshima ruins in March and April 1946, by Daniel A. McGovern and Harry Mimura</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A member of the U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strategic_Bombing_Survey" title="Strategic Bombing Survey">Strategic Bombing Survey</a>, Lieutenant Daniel McGovern, used a film crew to document the results in early 1946.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greg_Mitchell_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greg_Mitchell-256">[256]</a></sup> The film crew's work resulted in a three-hour documentary entitled <i>The Effects of the Atomic Bombs Against Hiroshima and Nagasaki</i>. The documentary included images from hospitals showing the human effects of the bomb; it showed burned out buildings and cars, and rows of skulls and bones on the ground. It was classified "secret" for the next 22 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore199573_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore199573-257">[257]</a></sup> During this time in America, it was a common practice for editors to keep graphic images of death out of films, magazines, and newspapers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeinSelden199725.E2.80.9326_252-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeinSelden199725.E2.80.9326-252">[252]</a></sup> The total of 90,000 ft (27,000 m) of film shot by McGovern's cameramen had not been fully aired as of 2009. According to <a href="/wiki/Greg_Mitchell" title="Greg Mitchell">Greg Mitchell</a>, with the 2004 documentary film <i><a href="/wiki/Original_Child_Bomb" title="Original Child Bomb">Original Child Bomb</a></i>, a small part of that footage managed to reach part of the American public "in the unflinching and powerful form its creators intended".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greg_Mitchell_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greg_Mitchell-256">[256]</a></sup></p>
<p>Motion picture company Nippon Eigasha started sending cameramen to Nagasaki and Hiroshima in September 1945. On October 24, 1945, a <a href="/wiki/Military_Police_Corps_(United_States)" title="Military Police Corps (United States)">U.S. military policeman</a> stopped a Nippon Eigasha cameraman from continuing to film in Nagasaki. All Nippon Eigasha's reels were then confiscated by the American authorities. These reels were in turn requested by the Japanese government, declassified, and saved from oblivion. Some black-and-white motion pictures were released and shown for the first time to Japanese and American audiences in the years from 1968 to 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greg_Mitchell_256-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greg_Mitchell-256">[256]</a></sup> The public release of film footage of the city post-attack, and some research about the human effects of the attack, was restricted during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occupied_Japan" title="Occupied Japan">occupation of Japan</a>, and much of this information was censored until the signing of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Peace_Treaty" title="San Francisco Peace Treaty">San Francisco Peace Treaty</a> in 1951, restoring control to the Japanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIshikawaSwain19815_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIshikawaSwain19815-258">[258]</a></sup></p>
<p>Only the most politically charged and detailed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effects_of_nuclear_weapons" title="Effects of nuclear weapons">weapons effects information</a> was censored during this period. The Hiroshima-based magazine, <i>Chugoku Bunka</i> for example, in its first issue published March 10, 1946, devotes itself to detailing the damage from the bombing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup> Similarly, there was no censorship of the factually written witness accounts, the book <i><a href="/wiki/Hiroshima_(book)" title="Hiroshima (book)">Hiroshima</a></i> written by <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a> winner <a href="/wiki/John_Hersey" title="John Hersey">John Hersey</a>, which was originally published in article form in the popular magazine <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-260">[260]</a></sup> on August 31, 1946, is reported to have reached Tokyo in English by January 1947, and the translated version was released in Japan in 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharp2000434.E2.80.93452_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharp2000434.E2.80.93452-262">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_New_Yorker_2010_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_Yorker_2010-263">[263]</a></sup> The book narrates the stories of the lives of six bomb survivors from immediately prior to, and months after, the dropping of the Little Boy bomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_260-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-260">[260]</a></sup> Beginning in 1974 a compilation of drawings and artwork made by the survivors of the bombings began to be compiled, with completion in 1977 and under both book and exhibition format, it was titled <i>The Unforgettable Fire</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unforgettable_Fire_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-Unforgettable_Fire-264">[264]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-attack_casualties">Post-attack casualties</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Epidemiology_data_for_low-linear_energy_transfer_radiation" title="Epidemiology data for low-linear energy transfer radiation">Epidemiology data for low-linear energy transfer radiation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_5" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:640px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_6" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Medical_aspect%2C_Hiroshima%2C_Japan%2C_1946-03-23%2C_342-USAF-11034.ogv/640px--Medical_aspect%2C_Hiroshima%2C_Japan%2C_1946-03-23%2C_342-USAF-11034.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:640px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="542.08" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Medical_aspect,_Hiroshima,_Japan,_1946-03-23,_342-USAF-11034.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/78/Medical_aspect%2C_Hiroshima%2C_Japan%2C_1946-03-23%2C_342-USAF-11034.ogv/Medical_aspect%2C_Hiroshima%2C_Japan%2C_1946-03-23%2C_342-USAF-11034.ogv.480p.webm" type="video/webm; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Medical_aspect,_Hiroshima,_Japan,_1946-03-23,_342-USAF-11034.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Silent film footage taken in Hiroshima in March 1946 showing survivors with severe burns and <a href="/wiki/Keloid" title="Keloid">keloid</a> scars. Although most all survivors with burns also had burns compounded by the conventional fires that were lit after the bombing, this footage was primarily made with the survivors being asked to stand in the orientation they were in at the time of the nuclear thermal/<i>flash</i>, to document and convey the relatively unique <a href="/wiki/Line-of-sight_propagation" title="Line-of-sight propagation">line-of-sight</a> nature of <a href="/wiki/Flash_burn" title="Flash burn">flash burns</a>, and to show that, much like a <a href="/wiki/Sunburn" title="Sunburn">sunburn</a>, thick clothing and fabric offered complete protection in many cases. The sometimes extensive <a href="/wiki/Burn_scar_contracture" title="Burn scar contracture">burn scar contracture</a> that is also seen particularly around joints, is not unusual, being common to all <a href="/wiki/Burn#Signs_and_symptoms" title="Burn">2nd and 3rd degree burns</a> when they cover a large area of skin. The film also features a small number of completely unburnt survivors who were included in the film to show the recovery made by those whose <a href="/wiki/Radiation_therapy#Late_side_effects" title="Radiation therapy">hair had previously fallen out</a> from the effects of <a href="/wiki/Acute_radiation_syndrome" title="Acute radiation syndrome">acute radiation syndrome</a> but by the time of filming, they had recovered.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the spring of 1948, the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Bomb_Casualty_Commission" title="Atomic Bomb Casualty Commission">Atomic Bomb Casualty Commission</a> (ABCC) was established in accordance with a presidential directive from Truman to the <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" title="United States National Research Council">National Research Council</a> to conduct investigations of the late effects of radiation among the survivors in Hiroshima and Nagasaki.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup> One of the early studies conducted by the ABCC was on the outcome of pregnancies occurring in Hiroshima and Nagasaki, and in a <a href="/wiki/Controlling_for_a_variable" title="Controlling for a variable">control</a> city, <a href="/wiki/Kure,_Hiroshima" title="Kure, Hiroshima">Kure</a>, located 18 mi (29 km) south of Hiroshima, in order to discern the conditions and outcomes related to radiation exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup> Dr. <a href="/wiki/James_V._Neel" title="James V. Neel">James V. Neel</a> led the study which found that the number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birth_defects" title="Birth defects">birth defects</a> was not significantly higher among the children of survivors who were pregnant at the time of the bombings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup> Neel also studied the longevity of the children who survived the bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki, reporting that between 90 and 95 percent were still living 50 years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USA_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-USA-268">[268]</a></sup></p>
<p>The National Academy of Sciences questioned Neel's procedure which did not filter the Kure population for possible radiation exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnston2008143_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnston2008143-269">[269]</a></sup> Overall, while a <a href="/wiki/Statistical_significance" title="Statistical significance">statistically insignificant</a> increase in birth defects occurred directly after the bombings of Nagasaki and Hiroshima, Neel and others noted that in approximately 50 humans who were of an early <a href="/wiki/Gestational_age" title="Gestational age">gestational age</a> at the time of the bombing and who were all within about 1 km from the hypocenter, an increase in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microencephaly" title="Microencephaly">microencephaly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anencephaly" title="Anencephaly">anencephaly</a> was observed upon birth, with the incidence of these two particular malformations being nearly 3 times what was to be expected when compared to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Control_group" title="Control group">control group</a> in Kure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCormack200856_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCormack200856-270">[270]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1985, <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins University</a> human geneticist <a href="/wiki/James_F._Crow" title="James F. Crow">James F. Crow</a> examined Neel's research and confirmed that the number of birth defects was not significantly higher in Hiroshima and Nagasaki.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrimskyShorett2005118_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrimskyShorett2005118-272">[272]</a></sup> Many members of the ABCC and its successor <a href="/wiki/Radiation_Effects_Research_Foundation" title="Radiation Effects Research Foundation">Radiation Effects Research Foundation</a> (RERF) were still looking for possible birth defects or other causes among the survivors decades later, but found no evidence that they were more common among the survivors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USA_268-1"><a href="#cite_note-USA-268">[268]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup> Despite the insignificance of birth defects found in Neel's study and the detailed medical literature, up to 1987, historians such as <a href="/wiki/Ronald_E._Powaski" title="Ronald E. Powaski">Ronald E. Powaski</a> frequently wrote that Hiroshima experienced "an increase in stillbirths, birth defects, and infant mortality" following the atomic bomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowaski198727_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowaski198727-274">[274]</a></sup></p>
<p>As <a href="/wiki/Radiation-induced_cancer" title="Radiation-induced cancer">cancers do not immediately emerge after exposure to radiation</a> instead radiation-induced cancer has a minimum <a href="/wiki/Radiation-induced_cancer" title="Radiation-induced cancer">latency period</a> of some 5+ years. An <a href="/wiki/Epidemiology" title="Epidemiology">epidemiology</a> study by the RERF estimates that from 1950 to 2000, 46% of leukemia deaths and 11% of solid cancers of unspecificed lethality, could be due to radiation from the bombs, with the statistical excess being 200 leukemia deaths and 1,700 solid cancers of undeclared lethality. Both of these statistics being derived from the observation of approximately half of the total survivors, strictly those who took part in the study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rerf-cancers_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-rerf-cancers-275">[275]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hibakusha"><i>Hibakusha</i></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hibakusha" title="Hibakusha">Hibakusha</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hibakujumoku" title="Hibakujumoku">Hibakujumoku</a></div>
<p>The survivors of the bombings are called <i><a href="/wiki/Hibakusha" title="Hibakusha">hibakusha</a></i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span lang="ja">被爆者</span>, <small>Japanese pronunciation: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Japanese" title="Help:IPA/Japanese">[çibakɯ̥ɕa]</a></span>)</span>, a Japanese word that literally translates to "explosion-affected people". The Japanese government has recognized about 650,000 people as <i>hibakusha</i>. As of March 31, 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 164,621 are still alive, mostly in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup> The government of Japan recognizes about 1% of these as having illnesses caused by radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup> The memorials in Hiroshima and Nagasaki contain lists of the names of the <i>hibakusha</i> who are known to have died since the bombings. Updated annually on the anniversaries of the bombings, as of August 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the memorials record the names of almost 485,000 <i>hibakusha</i>; 308,725 in Hiroshima<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup> and 175,743 in Nagasaki.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:1008px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NagasakiHypocentre.jpg" title="Panoramic view of the monument marking the hypocenter, or ground zero, of the atomic bomb explosion over Nagasaki"><img alt="A rectangular column rises above a dark stone base with Japanese writing on it. It sits atop a grass mound with is surrounded by alternating circles of stone path and grass. The is a wall around the whole monument, and bushes beyond." data-file-height="900" data-file-width="3714" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/NagasakiHypocentre.jpg/1000px-NagasakiHypocentre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/NagasakiHypocentre.jpg/1500px-NagasakiHypocentre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/NagasakiHypocentre.jpg/2000px-NagasakiHypocentre.jpg 2x" width="1000"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NagasakiHypocentre.jpg" title="File:NagasakiHypocentre.jpg"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Panoramic view of the monument marking the hypocenter, or ground zero, of the atomic bomb explosion over Nagasaki</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Hibakusha</i> and their children were (and still are) victims of severe discrimination in Japan due to public ignorance about the consequences of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiation_sickness" title="Radiation sickness">radiation sickness</a>, with much of the public believing it to be hereditary or even contagious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup> This is despite the fact that no statistically demonstrable increase of birth defects or congenital malformations was found among the later conceived children born to survivors of Hiroshima and Nagasaki.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup> A study of the long-term psychological effects of the bombings on the survivors found that even 17–20 years after the bombings had occurred survivors showed a higher prevalence of <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a> and <a href="/wiki/Somatization" title="Somatization">somatization</a> symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Double_survivors">Double survivors</span></h3>
<p>On March 24, 2009, the Japanese government officially recognized <a href="/wiki/Tsutomu_Yamaguchi" title="Tsutomu Yamaguchi">Tsutomu Yamaguchi</a> as a double <i>hibakusha</i>. He was confirmed to be 3 km (1.9 mi) from ground zero in Hiroshima on a business trip when Little Boy was detonated. He was seriously burnt on his left side and spent the night in Hiroshima. He arrived at his home city of Nagasaki on August 8, the day before Fat Man was dropped, and he was exposed to residual radiation while searching for his relatives. He was the first officially recognized survivor of both bombings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup> He died on January 4, 2010, at the age of 93, after a battle with stomach cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2006 documentary <i>Twice Survived: The Doubly Atomic Bombed of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</i> documented 165 <i>nijū hibakusha (lit. double explosion-affected people)</i>, and was screened at the United Nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Twice_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-Twice-285">[285]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Korean_survivors">Korean survivors</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Anti-Korean_sentiment#Japan" title="Anti-Korean sentiment">Anti-Korean sentiment § Japan</a></div>
<p>During the war, Japan brought as many as 670,000 Korean conscripts to Japan to work as <a href="/wiki/Japanese_war_crimes#Forced_labor" title="Japanese war crimes">forced labor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGruhl2007111_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGruhl2007111-286">[286]</a></sup> About 5,000–8,000 Koreans were killed in Hiroshima and another 1,500–2,000 died in Nagasaki.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dower1996_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dower1996-287">[287]</a></sup> For many years, Korean survivors had a difficult time fighting for the same recognition as <i>Hibakusha</i> as afforded to all Japanese survivors, a situation which resulted in the denial of the free health benefits to them. Most issues were eventually addressed in 2008 through lawsuits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Debate_over_bombings">Debate over bombings</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Debate_over_the_atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Debate over the atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">Debate over the atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A-Bomb_Dome.jpg"><img alt="A shot along a river. There is a bridge in the distance, and a ruined domed building in the middle distance. People walk along the footpath that runs parallel to the river." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="698" data-file-width="1007" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/A-Bomb_Dome.jpg/220px-A-Bomb_Dome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/A-Bomb_Dome.jpg/330px-A-Bomb_Dome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/A-Bomb_Dome.jpg/440px-A-Bomb_Dome.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A-Bomb_Dome.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Citizens of Hiroshima walk by the Hiroshima Peace Memorial, the closest building to have survived the city's atomic bombing.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The role of the bombings in <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">Japan's surrender</a> and the U.S.'s ethical justification for them has been the subject of scholarly and popular debate for decades. J. Samuel Walker wrote in an April 2005 overview of recent historiography on the issue, "the controversy over the use of the bomb seems certain to continue." He wrote that "The fundamental issue that has divided scholars over a period of nearly four decades is whether the use of the bomb was necessary to achieve victory in the war in the Pacific on terms satisfactory to the United States."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker2005334_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker2005334-289">[289]</a></sup></p>
<p>Supporters of the bombings generally assert that they caused the Japanese surrender, preventing casualties on both sides during Operation Downfall. One figure of speech, "One hundred million [subjects of the Japanese Empire] will die for the Emperor and Nation",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJowettAndrew200223.E2.80.9324_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJowettAndrew200223.E2.80.9324-290">[290]</a></sup> served as a unifying slogan, although that phrase was intended as a figure of speech along the lines of the "<a href="/wiki/Ten_thousand_years" title="Ten thousand years">ten thousand years</a>" phrase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooksStanley200741.E2.80.9344_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooksStanley200741.E2.80.9344-6">[6]</a></sup> In Truman's 1955 <i>Memoirs</i>, "he states that the atomic bomb probably saved half a million U.S. lives—anticipated casualties in an Allied invasion of Japan planned for November. Stimson subsequently talked of saving one million U.S. casualties, and Churchill of saving one million American and half that number of British lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeldenSelden1990xxx.E2.80.93xxxi_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeldenSelden1990xxx.E2.80.93xxxi-291">[291]</a></sup> Scholars have pointed out various alternatives that could have ended the war without an invasion, but these alternatives could have resulted in the deaths of many more Japanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker199097.E2.80.93114_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker199097.E2.80.93114-292">[292]</a></sup> Supporters also point to an order given by the Japanese War Ministry on August 1, 1944, ordering the execution of Allied prisoners of war when the POW camp was in the combat zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[293]</a></sup></p>
<p>Those who oppose the bombings cite a number of reasons for their view, among them: a belief that atomic bombing is fundamentally immoral, that the bombings counted as <a href="/wiki/War_crime" title="War crime">war crimes</a>, that they were militarily unnecessary, that they constituted state terrorism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStohl1979279.5D_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStohl1979279.5D-294">[294]</a></sup> and that they involved racism against and the dehumanization of the Japanese people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup> The bombings were part of an already fierce conventional bombing campaign. This, together with the naval blockade, could also have eventually led to a Japanese surrender. At the time the United States dropped its atomic bomb on Nagasaki on August 9, 1945, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Manchuria" title="Soviet invasion of Manchuria">Soviet Union launched a surprise attack</a> with 1.6 million troops against the Kwantung Army in <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>. "The Soviet entry into the war", argued Japanese historian <a href="/wiki/Tsuyoshi_Hasegawa" title="Tsuyoshi Hasegawa">Tsuyoshi Hasegawa</a>, "played a much greater role than the atomic bombs in inducing Japan to surrender because it dashed any hope that Japan could terminate the war through Moscow's mediation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another popular view among critics of the bombings, originating with <a href="/wiki/Gar_Alperovitz" title="Gar Alperovitz">Gar Alperovitz</a> in 1965 and becoming the default position in Japanese school history textbooks, is the idea of atomic diplomacy: that the United States used nuclear weapons in order to intimidate the Soviet Union in the early stages of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomasMorgan-Witts1977391.E2.80.93392-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomasMorgan-Witts1977391.E2.80.93392_132-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFThomasMorgan-Witts1977">Thomas &amp; Morgan-Witts 1977</a>, pp. 391–392.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomasMorgan-Witts1977414-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomasMorgan-Witts1977414_133-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFThomasMorgan-Witts1977">Thomas &amp; Morgan-Witts 1977</a>, p. 414.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomasMorgan-Witts1977415-134"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomasMorgan-Witts1977415_134-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFThomasMorgan-Witts1977">Thomas &amp; Morgan-Witts 1977</a>, p. 415.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen19692566-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen19692566_135-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFAllen1969">Allen 1969</a>, p. 2566.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEKerr_et_al200542.E2.80.9343-136"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKerr_et_al200542.E2.80.9343_136-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKerr_et_al200542.E2.80.9343_136-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKerr_et_al200542.E2.80.9343_136-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFKerr_et_al2005">Kerr et al 2005</a>, pp. 42–43.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Malik-137"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Malik_137-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Malik, John (September 1985). <a class="external text" href="http://www.osti.gov/manhattan-project-history/publications/LANLHiroshimaNagasakiYields.pdf" rel="nofollow">"The Yields of the Hiroshima and Nagasaki Explosions"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>. Los Alamos National Laboratory<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">March 9,</span> 2014</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=The+Yields+of+the+Hiroshima+and+Nagasaki+Explosions&amp;rft.pub=Los+Alamos+National+Laboratory&amp;rft.date=1985-09&amp;rft.aulast=Malik&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.osti.gov%2Fmanhattan-project-history%2Fpublications%2FLANLHiroshimaNagasakiYields.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAtomic+bombings+of+Hiroshima+and+Nagasaki"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> describes how various values were recorded for the B-29's altitude at the moment of bomb release over Hiroshima. The strike report said 30,200 ft, the official history said 31,600 ft, Commander Parson's log entry was 32,700 ft, and the navigator's log was 31,060 ft—the latter possibly an error transposing two digits. A later calculation using the indicated atmospheric pressure arrived at the figure of 32,200 ft.<br/>
Similarly, several values have been reported as the altitude of the Little Boy bomb at the moment of detonation. Published sources vary in the range of 1,800 to 2,000 ft (550 to 610 m) above the city. The device was set to explode at 1,885 ft (575 m), but this was approximate. Malik uses the figure of 1,903 ft (580 m) plus or minus 50 ft (15 m), determined after data review by <a href="#CITEREFHubbell_et_al1969">Hubbell et al 1969</a>. Radar returns from the tops of multistory buildings near the hypocenter may have triggered the detonation at a somewhat higher altitude than planned. Kerr et al. (2005) found that a detonation altitude of 600 m (1,968.5 ft), plus or minus 20 m (65.6 ft), gave the best fit for all the measurement discrepancies.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-138"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-138">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20100624065430/http://www.cfo.doe.gov/me70/manhattan/hiroshima.htm" rel="nofollow">"The Atomic Bombing of Hiroshima, Aug 6, 1945"</a>. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">United States Department of Energy</a>. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.cfo.doe.gov/me70/manhattan/hiroshima.htm" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on June 24, 2010<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 25,</span> 2010</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=The+Atomic+Bombing+of+Hiroshima%2C+Aug+6%2C+1945&amp;rft.pub=United+States+Department+of+Energy&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.cfo.doe.gov%2Fme70%2Fmanhattan%2Fhiroshima.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAtomic+bombings+of+Hiroshima+and+Nagasaki"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
